Wasiyyah — Bequest
beginner
6 min
Definition and Ruling of the Will (Wasiya)
Defining wasiya and ruling of recommendation with less than one-third

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define wasiya in Hanafi law and state the ruling for bequeathing less than one-third, including when it is recommended to make no will.

Core Idea
A will transfers ownership after death; less than one-third is recommended, but leaving no will is better when heirs are poor.

This lesson introduces the chapter on wills (wasaya) from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya. It defines a will as a transfer of ownership effective after death and explains the recommended practice of bequeathing less than one-third of one's estate. The commentary from al-Hidayah provides guidance on when it is better to abandon the will altogether.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal definition of wasiya
From the text: wasiya is a tamlik (transfer of ownership) added to after death, by way of donation (tabarru'). It is an obligation (ijab) that takes effect after death.
Hanafis emphasize that it is a transfer of ownership, not just a recommendation.
2
Understand the four categories of wills
Wills are divided into obligatory (e.g., for missed zakat), permissible (to a rich person), disliked (to sinners), and recommended. The passage focuses on the recommended type.
The author begins with the recommended type.
3
Analyze the recommended amount
It is recommended to bequeath less than one-third, even if heirs are wealthy. Reason: by reducing the bequest, the testator leaves more wealth for relatives, which is a form of charity.
The full one-third is the maximum, not the recommended amount.
4
Consider the better option: will or no will?
If the heirs are poor and need the inheritance, it is better to abandon the will entirely. This allows the wealth to go directly to needy relatives, which is the best charity as per the hadith: 'The best charity is to the relative who is hostile.'
This overrides the general recommendation to will less than one-third.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

هي إيجاب بعد الموت، وندبت بأقل من الثلث عند غنى ورثته
[1] قوله: كتاب الوصايا؛ لا يخفى مناسبة إيراد كتاب الوصايا في آخر الكتاب؛ لأن آخر أحوال الآدمي في الدنيا الموت والوصية معاملة وقت الموت، وله اختصاص بكتاب الجنايات والديات إذ الجناية قد تفضي إلى الموت الذي وقته وقت الوصية. ثم اعلم أن الوصايا جمع وصية، والوصية: اسم بمعنى المصدر، لما سمي الموصى به وصية، وهي في الشرع تمليك مضاف إلى ما بعد الموت بطريق التبرع. كذا في «العناية».
[2] قوله: هي... الخ؛ الوصية أربعة أقسام: الأول واجبة: كالوصية بالزكاة وفدية الصلاة وفدية الصوم التي فرط فيها. والثاني: مباحة كالوصية للغني. والثالث: مكروهة؛ كالوصية لأهل الفسوق. والرابع: مستحبة. ابتدأ المصنف بالقسم الرابع حيث قال: وندبت... الخ.
[3] قوله: وندبت... الخ؛ أي ندبت الوصية بأقل من الثلث، ولو عند غناء ورثته لما قال في «الهداية»: ويستحب أن يوصي بدون الثلث سواء كانت الورثة أغنياء أو فقراء؛ لأن في التنقيص صلة القريب بترك ماله عليهم، بخلاف استكماله الثلث؛ لأنه استيفاء تمام حقه، فلا صلة. ثم هل الوصية بأقل من الثلث أولى أم تركها؟ قالوا: إن كانت الورثة فقراء ولا يستغنون بما يرثون، فالترك أولى لما فيه من الصدقة على القريب، وقد قال: «أفضل الصدقة على ذي الرحم الكاشح».
Chapter of Wills
It is an obligation after death, and it is recommended with less than one-third when his heirs are wealthy.
[1] His statement: 'Chapter of Wills'; the appropriateness of placing the Chapter of Wills at the end of the book is not hidden, because the last state of a human in the world is death, and the will is a transaction at the time of death, and it has connection to the chapter of injuries and blood-money because injury may lead to death, the time of which is the time of the will. Then know that wasaya is the plural of wasiya, and wasiya is a noun in the sense of the verbal noun, because the thing bequeathed is called wasiya, and it is in the Sharia: a transfer of ownership (tamlik) added to after death by way of donation. Thus in al-'Inayah.
[2] His statement: 'It is...' etc.; the will (wasiya) has four types: first, obligatory: like willing the zakat and fidyah for prayer and fidyah for fasting which he neglected. Second, permissible: like willing to a rich person. Third, disliked: like willing to people of transgression. Fourth, recommended. The author began with the fourth type where he said: 'it is recommended' etc.
[3] His statement: 'and it is recommended...' etc.; meaning the will is recommended with less than one-third, even when his heirs are wealthy because of what he said in al-Hidayah: 'It is recommended to will less than one-third, whether the heirs are rich or poor; because reducing [the bequest] is connecting relatives by leaving one's wealth to them, contrary to completing one-third, because that is taking full right, so no connection. Then, is willing less than one-third better, or abandoning it? They said: If the heirs are poor and not independent with what they inherit, then abandoning it is better because it contains charity to the relative, and it was said: 'The best charity is to the relative who is hostile.'
Source pages p. 580

Commentary
This passage introduces the chapter on wills (wasaya) in Hanafi fiqh. It defines a will as 'an obligation after death' (ijab ba'd al-mawt) and states that it is recommended (nudibat) to bequeath less than one-third of one's estate when the heirs are wealthy. The commentary explains that wills are placed at the end of the book because they concern the time of death. It defines wasiya as a transfer of ownership that takes effect after death, by donation. The commentary then categorizes wills into four types: obligatory (e.g., making up missed zakat or fasting), permissible (to a rich person), disliked (to sinful people), and recommended (the topic here). The author starts with the recommended type. The preferred amount is less than one-third, even if heirs are rich, because limiting the bequest allows keeping more for relatives, whereas using the full one-third is taking one's full right and does not constitute charity. However, if the heirs are poor and need the inheritance, it is better to leave no will at all, because any charity to non-relatives is inferior to leaving wealth to needy relatives. This is based on the hadith 'The best charity is to the relative who is hostile.'

Key Terms
وصية (Wasiya): A transfer of ownership (tamlik) that is added to (mufawwad) after death, by way of donation (tabarru').
ثلث (Thuluth): One-third of the estate, the maximum amount that can be bequeathed without the permission of the heirs.
ندبت (Nudibat): It is recommended (mustahabb); indicates a desirable but non-obligatory act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Hanafi law, when is it better to NOT make any will (wasiya)?
A) When the heirs are wealthy
B) When the heirs are poor and need the inheritance
C) When the testator has many debts
D) When the testator is in good health

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a will is a deferred ownership transfer; bequeathing less than one-third is recommended, but if heirs are poor, it is better to leave no will and let them inherit.

Wasiyyah — Bequest
intermediate
10 min
Preference of Making a Will and Its Validity for a Fetus
Ruling on when to bequeath and validity for unborn

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when making a will is recommended versus leaving it based on the heirs' financial status, and explain why a will for an unborn child is valid in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
A will is better when heirs are wealthy; leaving it is better when they are poor. A will for a fetus is valid because it resembles inheritance.

The Hanafi school teaches that the recommendation to make a will (wasiyyah) is not absolute but depends on the financial condition of the heirs. This lesson examines when it is more virtuous to make a will and when it is better to leave the property to be inherited. Additionally, we explore the validity of a will for an unborn child (al-haml), a ruling that may seem counterintuitive given the fetus's inability to accept, but is justified by analogy to inheritance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios regarding heirs' wealth
The passage distinguishes between heirs who are wealthy or become wealthy through the inheritance, and those who are poor and do not become wealthy. Identify the ruling for each case.
Wealth is defined by owning a specific amount, such as 4,000 dirhams per heir according to one narration.
2
Determine the ruling for each scenario
If heirs are wealthy, making a will is superior because it becomes charity to a stranger. If they are poor, leaving the will is superior because it provides for relatives.
The recommendation is not binary; there are degrees of preferability.
3
Understand the intra-madhhab difference for minor heirs
When the heirs are minor children and poor, leaving the will is absolutely better according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn), while Abu Hanifa's general rule applies when children are adults.
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) follows Sahibayn for minor heirs.
4
Recognize the validity of a will for a fetus
A will made for an unborn child is valid in the Hanafi school. The fetus can be a legatee because the will resembles a delegation (istikhlaf) and the fetus is eligible to inherit.
The objection that the fetus cannot accept is overcome by noting that a will combines aspects of gift (requiring acceptance) and inheritance (not requiring acceptance); here the inheritance aspect prevails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

«الهداية».

(۳) في رد المحتار » (٦ : ٦٥٢).
Or their self-sufficiency by their share, like leaving it without either, and it is valid for the fetus. I mean if the heirs are not wealthy and do not become wealthy by their share from the estate, then leaving the will is better. It is valid for the fetus. And if they are wealthy or become wealthy by their share, then the will is more virtuous because it is charity to a stranger, while leaving is a gift to a relative, and the former is more virtuous because one seeks by it the face of Allah, may He be exalted. It is said in this aspect one is given a choice because each involves virtue: charity or maintaining ties. End of the speech of al-Hidayah. Its summary is that one should not bequeath the entire third, rather it is recommended to reduce it absolutely because the Prophet (peace be upon him) considered the third much by his saying: 'And the third is much.' However, reducing it due to the poverty of the heirs, although recommended, there is something more virtuous than it, which is leaving it entirely, for the recommended has varying degrees, and likewise the sunnah, the disliked, and others. In al-Qahustani: If the wealth is little, one should not make a will according to what Abu Hanifa said, and this is if the children are adults; if they are minors, then leaving is absolutely better according to what is narrated from the two shaykhs, as in Qadikhan. He said in al-Hawi al-Qudsi: Whoever has no heir and no debt upon him, it is more virtuous to bequeath all his wealth after giving charity with his hand. This is the summary from Radd al-Muhtar. [1] The saying: 'Or their self-sufficiency by their share' means their becoming wealthy, such that each inherits four thousand dirhams according to what is reported from the Imam, or inherits ten thousand dirhams according to what is reported from al-Fadli. Qahustani from al-Zahiriya, and al-Atqani restricted it to the former. Thus said al-Shami. [2] The saying: 'And it is valid for the fetus' because it is a delegation in a sense, as he makes him a deputy in some of his wealth, and the fetus is suitable to be a deputy in inheritance; similarly in the will. It cannot be said that its condition is acceptance, and the fetus is not qualified for it, because it resembles a gift and inheritance. Due to its resemblance to a gift, acceptance is required if possible, and its resemblance to inheritance renders it unnecessary if not possible, acting on both resemblances. Hence it is voided by the death of the legatee before acceptance. Thus in al-Shami.
Source pages p. 581

Commentary
This passage from a commentary on al-Wiqayah discusses the ruling on making a will (wasiyyah) and its preference based on the financial state of the heirs. It first explains that if the heirs are not wealthy and will not become wealthy from their inheritance share, then it is better not to make a will (ترك الوصية). However, if the heirs are already wealthy or will become wealthy from the inheritance, then making a will is more virtuous because it becomes charity to a non-relative, whereas leaving it is a gift to a relative. The author notes that giving charity to a non-relative seeking Allah's face is superior. The passage then summarizes from al-Hidayah that one should not bequeath the full third, but rather reduce it, because the Prophet said 'the third is much.' However, if the heirs are poor, reducing the bequest is recommended, but leaving it entirely is even better. It cites al-Qahustani that if the wealth is little, one should not make a will according to Abu Hanifa when the children are adults, but if they are minors, leaving the will is absolutely better according to the two shaykhs (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). For a person with no heirs and no debts, it is better to bequeath all wealth after giving some charity in life. The passage also addresses the validity of a will for an unborn child (الحمل), stating it is valid because it resembles a delegation (استخلاف), and the fetus is eligible to inherit. The objection that the fetus cannot accept is rebutted by noting that a will resembles both a gift (requiring acceptance) and inheritance (not requiring acceptance); thus, if acceptance is possible, it is required, but if not (as with a fetus), it is waived. The will is voided if the legatee dies before acceptance.

Key Terms
الوصية (al-wasiyyah): A bequest or will directing disposal of property after death up to one-third of the estate.
الحمل (al-haml): An unborn child; in the context of wills, one who is in the womb at the time of the will.
الثُّلث (al-thuluth): One-third: the maximum amount that can be bequeathed without heirs' consent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is a will for an unborn child valid despite the fetus's inability to accept?
A) The fetus is considered a legal person from conception.
B) The will resembles inheritance, which does not require acceptance.
C) The guardian accepts on behalf of the fetus.
D) The Prophet (peace be upon him) specifically allowed it.

Key Takeaway
The recommendation to make a will depends on the heirs' financial status: wealthy heirs make bequests superior; poor heirs make leaving the will superior; and a will for an unborn child is valid due to its similarity to inheritance.

Conditions for a Will to an Unborn Child and Exclusion of the Fetus
Ruling with proof on conditions for a will to a fetus and validity of istithna'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi conditions for a valid will to an unborn child and explain the permissibility of excluding the fetus from a bequest using the principle of individual contractibility.

Core Idea
A will for an unborn child is valid only if born within six months; excluding the fetus from a bequest is valid because it can be independently contracted.

This lesson examines two related rulings from the Hanafi school on wills involving unborn children. The first sets the condition for a valid bequest in favour of a fetus. The second confirms that excluding the fetus from a bequest of its mother (a slave-girl) is permissible, based on a general legal principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings discussed
The passage deals with: (a) validity of a will to an unborn child with a time condition, (b) validity of excluding the fetus from a bequest of the mother.
2
Analyse the proof for the first ruling — qiyas to inheritance
The commentary argues that because the fetus can inherit, it can also be a legatee by analogy. Identify the asl (inheritance) and far' (will).
3
Apply the principle of individual contractibility to the second ruling
The general principle: whatever can be independently contracted can be excluded from a contract. Since a will for the fetus is valid, excluding it from a bequest is valid.
4
Note the cross-madhhab difference
The Shafi'i school does not require the six-month condition; they require only live birth. Understand the practical consequence.
5
Formulate the operative Hanafi rule
The mufta bihi: a will to an unborn child is valid only if born within six months of the will; excluding the fetus from a bequest is valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبه إن ولدت لأقل من مدتها من وقتها، وهي والاستثناء في وصية بأمة إلا حملها، [1] قوله : وبه ; أي بالحمل ; لأنه يجري فيه الإرث، فيجري فيه الوصية أيضاً ; لأنها أخته. كذا في الزيلعي (2).
[2] قوله : وهي والاستثناء ; أي تصح الوصية بالأمة، واستثناء الحمل، كما لو قال : هذه الأمة لفلان إلا حملها، لما تقرر في الأصول : كلّ ما يصح إفراده بالعقد يصح استثناؤه من العقد، فإذا صح الوصية بالحمل، صح استثناء الحمل من الوصية.
And by it: if she gives birth at less than her term from its time. And it (the will) and the exclusion in a bequest of a slave-girl except her fetus. [1] The saying: 'And by it' meaning by the fetus, because inheritance applies to it, so will also applies to it, because it is its sister. Thus in al-Zayla'i (2). [2] The saying: 'And it and the exclusion' meaning the bequest of the slave-girl and the exclusion of the fetus is valid, as if he said: 'This slave-girl is for so-and-so except her fetus,' because it is established in principles: Whatever can be made the subject of a contract individually can be excluded from the contract. So if a will for the fetus is valid, then excluding the fetus from the will is valid.
Source pages p. 582

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on wills for unborn children. It first states the condition: the will is valid only if the child is born within less than six months from the time of the will (i.e., the full term of pregnancy). The commentator explains that by 'it' is meant the fetus, and since inheritance applies to the fetus (it can inherit), a will in its favor is also valid by analogy. The passage then addresses a separate issue: if someone makes a will for a slave-girl (أمة) and excludes her fetus, that exclusion is valid. This is because, according to legal principles, anything that can be independently contracted can be excluded from a contract. Since a will for the fetus is valid, excluding it from the bequest of the mother is also valid. The source cites al-Zayla'i for the first point and the general principle for the second.

Key Terms
الحمل (al-haml): Fetus; the unborn child which can be a legatee if born within six months of the will.
الاستثناء (al-istithna'): Exclusion; removing part of the subject matter from the contract or bequest.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the fetus cannot own property
B) Because the fetus can be the subject of a will
C) Because the mother consents
D) Because the testator can change his mind

Key Takeaway
A will to an unborn child requires birth within six months; excluding the fetus from a bequest is valid if the fetus could itself be a legatee.

Validity of Wills between Muslims and Dhimmis
Ruling with proof: wills between Muslim and dhimmi are valid both ways

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on wills between Muslims and dhimmis in both directions and cite the Quranic proof and the reasoning based on dhimma equality.

Core Idea
A will between a Muslim and a dhimmi is valid both ways — the dhimma contract ensures transactional equality.

Wills (wasiyyah) are a form of transactional kindness that extends beyond the Muslim community. This passage from the Hidayah clarifies that a Muslim may bequeath to a dhimmi, and a dhimmi may bequeath to a Muslim, based on clear Quranic permission and the legal parity established by the dhimma contract. It also draws a boundary: this rule does not apply to enemies at war (harbis) in their own territory, but does apply to those granted safe passage (musta'min).

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and Its Two Directions
The passage states that a will from a Muslim to a dhimmi and from a dhimmi to a Muslim are both valid. Identify these two cases.
The phrase 'wa 'aksuhu' means 'and its opposite'.
2
Examine the Quranic Proof for Muslim → Dhimmī
For the Muslim-to-dhimmi direction, the text cites Quran 60:8. Locate the verse and note how it permits kindness and justice toward non-hostile non-Muslims, which includes bequests.
The verse is a general permission; it is not restricted to charity but extends to all forms of kindness, including wills.
3
Understand the Reasoning for Dhimmī → Muslim
For the direction from dhimmi to Muslim, the proof is rational: the dhimma contract places dhimmis on equal footing with Muslims in transactions. Since gifts and charity (tasarru') are permitted during life, the same applies after death via wills.
This is a qiyas (analogy) from living transactions to posthumous ones.
4
Distinguish Between Dhimmī, Ḥarbī, and Musta'min
The passage adds that a will for a harbi in dar al-harb is not valid, but a musta'min (non-Muslim with safe passage) is treated like a dhimmi. Identify the three categories and their respective rulings.
The key distinction is the existence of a peace pact or safe conduct.
5
Note the Cross‑Madhhab Difference
The Hanafi position is fully reciprocal. Some other schools (e.g. Shafi'i) allow a Muslim to bequeath to a non-Muslim but may restrict the reverse. Recognize this ikhtilaf and its basis.
The thamarah (practical consequence) is that in Shafi'i law, a dhimmi's bequest to a Muslim might be invalid if the dhimmi is of an unprotected religion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن المسلم للذمي وعكسه 
[3] قوله : ومن المسلم للذمي وعكسه ; أي من الذمي للمسلم ; يعني تصح الوصية من الجانبين، أما الأوّل ; لقوله : لَا يَنهَنكُمُ اللَّهُ عَنِ الَّذِينَ لَمْ يُقَتِلُوكُمْ فِي الدِّينِ ) الآية(٣) ، وأما الثاني ; فلأنهم بعقد الذمة ساووا المسلمين في المعاملات ; ولذا جازَ التورّع من الجانبين في حالةِ الحياة، فكذا بعد الممات. كذا في «الهداية»(4). 
(٤) قوله : للحربي ; أي في داره لا تجوز إنّما قيدنا بداره ; لأن المستأمن كالذمي
And from the Muslim to the dhimmi and its opposite. [3] The saying: 'And from the Muslim to the dhimmi and its opposite' i.e. from the dhimmi to the Muslim, meaning: the will is valid from both sides. As for the first: because of His saying: 'Allah does not forbid you from those who do not fight you in religion' (Quran 60:8). And as for the second: because by the covenant of protection (dhimma) they are equal to Muslims in transactions; hence charity is permissible from both sides during life, so similarly after death. Thus in al-Hidayah (4). (4) The saying: 'For the harbi' i.e. in his abode it is not valid; we only qualified it with 'his abode' because the musta'min (one granted safe passage) is like the dhimmi.
Source pages p. 582

Commentary
This passage establishes the permissibility of wills between Muslims and dhimmis (protected non-Muslims) in both directions. It cites the Quranic verse 60:8, which permits kindness and justice towards non-Muslims who do not fight Muslims. For the reverse direction (dhimmi to Muslim), the reasoning is that the dhimma contract places dhimmis on equal footing with Muslims in transactional matters, including bequests. Therefore, since gifts and charity are allowed between them during life, wills are similarly allowed after death. The passage also clarifies that this ruling applies only to dhimmis, not to harbis (enemy combatants) who are in dar al-harb; for a harbi in their own territory, a will is not valid. However, a musta'min (a non-Muslim with safe passage) is treated like a dhimmi in this regard.

Key Terms
الذمي (al-dhimmi): A non-Muslim living under the protection of an Islamic state with a dhimma contract, enjoying rights and obligations.
الحربي (al-harbi): A non-Muslim who is in a state of war with Islam; a will in favor of a harbi in dar al-harb is not valid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a will from a Muslim to a dhimmi valid according to the passage?
A) Because of a hadith
B) Because of Quran verse 60:8
C) Because of consensus
D) Because of analogy with inheritance

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits wills between Muslims and dhimmis in both directions, citing Quran 60:8 and the principle of transactional equality under the dhimma contract.

Rulings on Will Involving Pregnant Slave, Exception of Fetus, and Will between Muslim and Dhimmi
Expanding three cases of valid wasiyyah with proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid will involving a pregnant slave, an exception of a fetus, and a will between a Muslim and a dhimmi, and explain the proofs for each.

Core Idea
A will for an unborn fetus is valid if born within six months; exception of a fetus from a will is valid; wills between Muslims and dhimmis are valid both ways.

The Hanafi school addresses several special cases of wasiyyah that test the boundaries of validity. This passage from the commentary on Al-Hidayah expands three such rulings: a will regarding a pregnant slave woman, the exception of her fetus from that will, and the mutual validity of wills between Muslims and dhimmis. Each ruling is supported by a clear legal principle or textual proof, demonstrating the school's systematic approach.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
Locate the three distinct rulings in the passage: (1) will for a pregnant slave woman, (2) exception of the fetus from the will, (3) will between Muslim and dhimmi from both sides.
2
Analyze the condition for the pregnant slave
The text states 'إن ولدت لأقل من ستة أشهر من الوصية' (if she gives birth in less than six months from the will). Understand why six months is the minimum period of pregnancy in Hanafi fiqh and that this is a condition for validity, not just a recommendation.
The proof is qiyas: inheritance applies to the fetus, so will applies to it as well.
3
Apply the usul principle for exception
Note the principle: 'كل ما يصح إفراده بالعقد يصح استثناؤه من العقد' (whatever is valid to be singled out by contract, its exception from the contract is valid). Since a will for the fetus is valid, excepting it from the will is also valid.
This is a general usul al-fiqh rule applied to wasiyyah.
4
Examine the dhimmi will ruling
Two directions: Muslim wills to dhimmi and dhimmi wills to Muslim. For the first, the proof is Quran 60:8 which does not forbid kindness to non-hostile non-Muslims. For the second, the dhimmi is equal to Muslims in transactions by the covenant of dhimma, so a will is valid just as a gift is valid during life.
This ruling is specific to dhimmis, not harbis (enemy non-Muslims).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبه إن ولدت لأقل من مدتِهِ من وقتها، وهي والاستثناء في وصيَّةٍ بأمةٍ إلا حملها، ومن المسلم للذمي وعكسه قوله : وبه؛ أي بالحمل؛ لأنه يجري فيه الإرث، فيجري فيه الوصية أيضاً. قوله : وهي والاستثناء؛ أي تصح الوصية بالأمة، واستثناء الحمل، كما لو قال : هذه الأمة لفلان إلا حملها، لما تقرر في الأصول : كلّ ما يصح إفراده بالعقد يصح استثناؤه من العقد، فإذا صح الوصية بالحمل، صح استثناء الحمل من الوصية. قوله : ومن المسلم للذمي وعكسه؛ أي من الذمي للمسلم؛ يعني تصح الوصية من الجانبين، أما الأوّل؛ لقوله : لَا يَنْهَاكُمُ اللَّهُ عَنِ الَّذِينَ لَمْ يُقَاتِلُوكُمْ فِي الدِّينِ الآية، وأما الثاني؛ فلأنهم بعقد الذمة ساووا المسلمين في المعاملات؛ ولذا جاز التورّع من الجانبين في حالةِ الحياة، فكذا بعد الممات. كذا في «الهداية».
And by it, if she gives birth in less than his period from his time, and it and the exception in a will regarding a slave woman except her fetus, and from Muslim to dhimmi and its opposite. He said: and by it; i.e., by the fetus; because inheritance applies to it, so will also applies. He said: it and the exception; i.e., the will regarding a slave woman and exception of the fetus is valid, as if he said: this slave woman belongs to so-and-so except her fetus, based on the principle established in usul: everything that is valid to be singled out by contract, its exception from the contract is valid, so if the will for the fetus is valid, the exception of the fetus from the will is valid. He said: and from Muslim to dhimmi and its opposite; i.e., from dhimmi to Muslim; meaning the will is valid from both sides. As for the first, due to His statement: 'Allah does not forbid you from those who did not fight you in religion' (Al-Mumtahina: 8). As for the second, because by the covenant of dhimma they are equal to Muslims in transactions; thus donation is permitted from both sides in life, so also after death. Thus in Al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 582

Commentary
This passage expands three rulings on the validity of wills. First, a will regarding a pregnant slave woman is valid only if she gives birth within six months of the will, because the minimum period of pregnancy is six months. The proof is qiyas: inheritance applies to the fetus, so a will for it is also valid. Second, if someone says 'my slave woman is for so-and-so except her fetus,' the exception is valid because whatever can be the object of a contract can be excepted from it. Since a will for the fetus is valid, excepting it from the will is also valid. Third, a will between a Muslim and a dhimmi is valid from both directions. For a Muslim willing to a dhimmi, the Quranic verse (Al-Mumtahina: 8) permits kind treatment. For a dhimmi willing to a Muslim, since the dhimmi is equal to Muslims in worldly transactions by the covenant of protection, a will is valid just as a gift is valid during life. These rulings are established in the relied-upon books of the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
حمل (haml): In this passage, the fetus in the womb of a slave woman, which can be the object of a will.
استثناء (istithna): Exception; here, exempting the fetus from the will of the mother slave woman.
ذمي (dhimmi): A non-Muslim subject living under Muslim rule with a covenant of protection; can receive or make a will.
مدته (muddatihi): His period; here refers to the minimum period of pregnancy (six months) in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) She must give birth within six months of the will
B) She must give birth after six months
C) She must be married
D) The will must be for a male child

Key Takeaway
A will for a pregnant slave is valid only if she delivers within six months; an exception of the fetus from such a will is valid; and wills between Muslims and dhimmis are valid in both directions.

Will Limitations: One-Third, Heirs, and Murderers
Stating the rule that a will cannot exceed one-third for non-heirs and is invalid for heirs and direct murderers unless heirs permit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three main restrictions on a will in Hanafi fiqh and explain the evidence and dispute regarding wills to murderers.

Core Idea
A will cannot exceed one-third for non-heirs, is invalid for heirs, and is invalid for direct murderers unless heirs permit.

This passage from Al-Hidayah's commentary lays out three fundamental limitations on the testator's freedom to bequeath. These limitations protect the rights of legal heirs and uphold the principles of justice and family harmony. We will examine each restriction, its proof, and the intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab disputes, particularly concerning the killer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three restrictions
List the three cases where a will is restricted according to the passage: (1) will to non-heir limited to one-third, (2) will to heir invalid, (3) will to direct killer invalid unless heirs permit.
These are the three pillars of the ruling.
2
Examine the proof for the one-third limit
The hadith 'one-third is much' and the right of heirs are cited. The limit protects heirs from deprivation.
Other schools also recognize this limit.
3
Analyze the prohibition of a will to an heir
The hadith 'No will for an heir' and the rationale of avoiding favoritism and severed kinship are given. Heirship is determined at death, not at the time of the will.
This is agreed upon across schools.
4
Study the killer exception and the dispute
The hadith 'No will for a killer' is cited. Distinguish direct killing (e.g., stabbing) from indirect (e.g., digging a well). Note the intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf holds it is always invalid; Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (Sahibayn) allow it with heirs' permission.
The passage adopts the Sahibayn's view as mufta bihi.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and practical consequence
The relied-upon position is that heirs' permission validates a will to a killer. This differs from Shafi'i (always valid) and from Abu Yusuf (never valid).
The practical effect: in Hanafi fatwa, heirs can choose to allow the killer to benefit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالثلث للأجنبي لا في أكثر منه، ولا لوارثه وقاتله مباشرة إلا بإجازة ورثته قوله : مباشرة؛ احتراز عن القتل تسبيباً: كحفر البئر، وعند الشافعي تجوز الوصية للقاتل، وعلى هذا الخلاف إذا أوصى لرجل ثم إنه قتل الموصى. كما أفاده الملا خسرو، قلت: وبه صرّح الحدّادي والزَّيْلَعِي وغيرهما، هكذا في «الدر المختار». قوله : وبالثلث للأجنبي... الخ؛ أي يصح الوصية بالثلث، لا في أكثر من الثلث لما مر من قوله: (الثلث كثير... الخ؛ ولأنه حق الورثة، وتمامه في «الهداية». قوله : ولا لوارثه؛ أي لا تصح الوصية لوارثه؛ لقوله: «إن الله تعالى أعطى كل ذي حق حقه ألا لا وصية للوارث»؛ ولأنه يتأذى البعض بإيثار البعض، ففي تجويزه قطعية الرحم، ويعتبر كونه وارثاً أو غير وارث وقت الموت لا وقت الوصية؛ لأنه تمليك مضاف إلى ما بعد الموت، وحكمه يثبت بعد الموت. كذا في «الهداية». قوله : وقاتله؛ عطف على: وارثه؛ أي لا تصح الوصية للقاتل عامداً كان أو خاطئاً بعد أن كان مباشراً بقوله: (لا وصية للقاتل»؛ ولأنه استعجل ما آجره الله تعالى فيحرم الوصية كما تحرم الميراث إلا بإجازة ورثته؛ أي لو أجازتها الورثة جاز عند الطرفين. وقال أبو يوسف: لا تجوز؛ لأن جنايته باقية والامتناع لأجلها، ولهما أن الامتناع بحق الورثة؛ لأن نفع بطلانها يعود إليهم كنفع بطلان الميراث؛ ولأنهم لا
And with one-third for a non-heir, not more than that, nor for his heir and his killer directly except with permission of his heirs. He said: directly; as a precaution against killing by causation like digging a well. And according to al-Shafi'i, the will for a killer is permissible, and upon this disagreement if he made a will for a man and then he killed the testator. As stated by Mulla Khusraw, I said: and al-Haddadi and al-Zayla'i and others explicitly stated so, thus in Al-Durr al-Mukhtar. He said: and with one-third for a non-heir... etc.; meaning the will for one-third is valid, not more than one-third due to what passed from his saying: 'one-third is much' etc.; and because it is the right of the heirs, its completion is in Al-Hidayah. He said: and nor for his heir; i.e., the will for his heir is not valid; due to his saying: 'Indeed Allah gave every right-holder his right, indeed no will for an heir'; and because some are harmed by preferring others, so in permitting it is severing of ties of kinship, and heirship is considered at time of death not at time of will; because it is a transfer added to after death, and its ruling is established after death. Thus in Al-Hidayah. He said: and his killer; conjunctive to his heir; i.e., the will for the killer is not valid, whether intentional or mistaken, as long as he was direct, due to his saying: 'no will for a killer'; and because he hastened what Allah delayed, so the will is prohibited just as inheritance is prohibited, except with permission of his heirs; i.e., if the heirs permit it, it is permissible according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad). Abu Yusuf said: it is not permissible; because his crime remains and the prohibition is for its sake. But the two said: the prohibition is due to the right of the heirs; because the benefit of its invalidity returns to them like the benefit of the invalidity of inheritance; and because they do not...
Source pages p. 583

Commentary
This passage expands three interrelated rulings on the limitations of a will. First, a will to a non-heir is valid only up to one-third of the estate; more than that requires the permission of the heirs. The evidence is the well-known hadith 'one-third is much' and the fact that the heirs have a right to the estate. Second, a will to an heir is invalid, based on the prophetic hadith 'No will for an heir.' The testator is not allowed to favor one heir over others, as this can cause discord and sever family ties. Heirship is determined at the time of death, not at the time of the will. Third, a will to a direct killer of the testator is invalid unless the other heirs permit it. This applies whether the killing was intentional or accidental, as long as it was direct (e.g., stabbing, not indirect like digging a well). The hadith 'No will for a killer' is cited. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf holds that the prohibition is due to the killer's crime, so even if the heirs permit, the will is invalid. However, Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (Sahibayn) hold that the prohibition is for the sake of the heirs, so their permission makes the will valid. The relied-upon position is that of the Sahibayn. There is also an inter-madhhab dispute: Al-Shafi'i permits a will to a killer without any condition, based on a different interpretation of the hadith.

Key Terms
ثلث (thulth): One-third of the estate, which is the maximum amount a person can bequeath to non-heirs in a will without heirs' consent.
وارث (warith): An heir entitled to inherit by share or asabah; a will to such a person is invalid unless other heirs permit.
قاتل (qatil): A person who directly kills the testator; even accidental killing prevents the killer from benefiting from the will unless heirs permit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, when is a will to a direct murderer valid?
A) It is never valid
B) It is valid only if the killer repents
C) It is valid if the other heirs permit it
D) It is always valid

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a will is restricted to one-third for non-heirs, prohibited for heirs, and for direct killers unless heirs permit, with the mufta bihi allowing heirs' permission to validate the killer's will.

The One-Third Limit for Wills to Non-Heirs
States the basic rule restricting will to one-third

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maximum permitted portion for a will to a non-heir and explain the proof from hadith and legal reasoning.

Core Idea
A will to a non-heir is valid only up to one-third of the estate.

This lesson introduces the foundational rule of Islamic inheritance law: you cannot bequeath more than one-third of your property to someone who is not a legal heir. This restriction protects the rights of the heirs and is based on a clear prophetic directive.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is writing a will (wasiyyah) for a non-heir (ajnabi). The ruling: it is valid (sahih) for up to one-third of the estate, but invalid beyond that unless the heirs consent.
The Arabic phrase: 'وبالثلث للأجنبي' establishes the one-third limit.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the Prophet's ﷺ statement 'الثلث كثير' (One-third is a lot), which is a hadith ahad with zanni thubut but qati dalalah in indicating the maximum limit.
This hadith is found in Sahih Bukhari and Muslim.
3
Understand the legal reasoning (wajh al-dalalah)
The Prophet ﷺ described one-third as 'a lot', implying that more than that is excessive and harms the heirs' rights. The remainder (two-thirds) belongs to the heirs by divine decree.
The commentary adds: 'ولأنه حق الورثة' (because it is the right of the heirs).
4
Note the consensus
This ruling is agreed upon (muttafaq alayh) among all schools of jurisprudence. There is no legitimate ikhtilaf on the one-third limit for non-heirs.
The Hanafi school treats it as a strict maximum; excess requires heirs' permission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالثلث للأجنبي لا في أكثر منه. [1] قوله: وبالثلث للأجنبي... الخ؛ أي يصح الوصية بالثلث، لا في أكثر من الثلث لما مر من قوله: (الثلث كثير... الخ)؛ ولأنه حق الورثة، وتمامه في «الهداية».
And with one-third for a non-heir, not more than that. [1] His saying: 'And with one-third for a non-heir... etc.': That is, a will is valid in one-third, not more than one-third, due to what has passed of his saying: 'One-third is a lot... etc.' and because it is the right of the heirs, and its completion is in Al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 583

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the matn. It establishes the foundational rule that a will (وصية) is valid only up to one-third of the deceased's estate for a non-heir. The commentary explains that the limit is based on the Prophet's hadith 'الثلث كثير' (One-third is a lot) and on the principle that the remaining two-thirds are the right of the legal heirs. This is a universally accepted ruling in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
الوصية (al-wasiyyah): A transfer of ownership attached to after death (تمليك مضاف إلى ما بعد الموت) as per later text.
الثلث (al-thuluth): One-third of the estate, the maximum permissible for a will to a non-heir.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) One-half
B) One-third
C) One-fourth
D) Any amount

Key Takeaway
A will to a non-heir is valid only up to one-third of the estate; anything more requires the consent of the legal heirs.

6 min
No Will for an Heir
Prohibits will for heir and clarifies timing of heir determination

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a will made for an heir and identify the point in time when heir status is determined for the prohibition.

Core Idea
A will for an heir is invalid because heirs already have fixed shares; the prohibition is based on the hadith 'No will for an heir'.

This lesson examines a foundational principle of Islamic inheritance law: a testator cannot make a will in favor of someone who is an heir under the law of succession. The prohibition stems from the hadith 'No will for an heir' and protects the fixed shares ordained by Allah. The passage also clarifies that the determining factor is the status at the time of death, not when the will is made.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is making a will (wasiyyah) for an heir. The ruling is that such a will is invalid (batil).
The passage uses 'لا تصح' meaning 'not valid'.
2
Trace the primary proof (dalil)
The immediate proof is the Prophet's hadith: 'Allah has given each right-holder his right; no will for an heir.' The negation 'لا وصية' indicates prohibition.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but its meaning is clear and accepted.
3
Understand the secondary proof (ratio legis)
The commentator adds that permitting a will for an heir would cause harm (by preferring some heirs over others) and sever family ties (qati'at al-rahim).
This shows the ruling is also supported by maqasid (preservation of family).
4
Determine the timing criterion
The passage states that the status of being an heir is considered at the time of death, not at the time of making the will. The reason is that a will is a transfer deferred until after death.
Thus, if a person is not an heir when making the will but becomes one at death, the will is invalid.
5
Apply to practical scenarios
If a testator leaves a will for someone who is not an heir at death (e.g., a non-relative), the will is valid. But if he leaves it for someone who is an heir at death, it is invalid unless other heirs consent after death.
Consent after death treats the bequest as a gift (hibah), not a will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا لوارثه. [2] قوله: ولا لوارثه؛ أي لا تصح الوصية لوارثه؛ لقوله: «إنّ الله تعالى أعطى كل ذي حق حقه ألا لا وصية للوارث»؛ ولأنه يتأذى البعض بإيثار البعض، ففي تجويزه قطعية الرحم، ويعتبر كونه وارثاً أو غير وارث وقت الموت لا وقت الوصية؛ لأنه تمليك مضاف إلى ما بعد الموت، وحكمه يثبت بعد الموت. كذا في «الهداية».
And not for his heir. [2] His saying: 'And not for his heir': That is, a will for his heir is not valid, due to his saying: 'Indeed Allah gave every right-holder his right; no will for an heir'; and because some are harmed by preferring others, so permitting it would sever ties of kinship, and consideration of being an heir or non-heir is at the time of death, not at the time of the will, because it is a transfer attached to after death, and its ruling is established after death. Thus in Al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 583

Commentary
This passage states the prohibition of making a will for an heir. The proof is the hadith: 'Allah has given each right-holder his right; no will for an heir.' The commentary explains that a will for an heir would cause harm and sever family ties. Importantly, the status of being an heir is determined at the time of death, not when the will is made, because a will is a transfer that takes effect after death. Thus, if someone is not an heir at the time of the will but becomes one at death, the will is invalid.

Key Terms
الوارث (al-warith): A person entitled to inherit from the deceased at the time of death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is the status of being an heir determined for the prohibition of a will?
A) At the time of making the will
B) At the time of death
C) At the time of acceptance
D) At the time of probate

Key Takeaway
A will for an heir is invalid; the heir's status is determined at death, not when the will is made.

No Will for a Direct Murderer - Exception with Heirs' Consent
Prohibits will for a direct killer; exception with heirs' consent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a will for a direct murderer, explain the dalīl and the exception of heirs' consent, and distinguish between direct and indirect killing.

Core Idea
A direct murderer's will is invalid but can be revived by heirs' consent.

This passage from ʿUmdat al-Riʿāya examines the validity of a will (waṣiyyah) made in favor of a person who directly killed the testator. The Hanafi school prohibits it based on a ḥadīth and analogy to inheritance, but introduces a key exception: if the other heirs consent, the will becomes valid. This lesson unpacks the ruling, the internal Hanafi disagreement, and the cross-madhhab contrast with the Shāfiʿī position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and core ruling
The passage rules on a will (waṣiyyah) made for a person who directly killed the testator. The basic ruling is that such a will is invalid (bāṭil).
This applies whether the killing was intentional or accidental, as long as it was direct.
2
Locate the dalīl
The commentator cites the hadith 'lā waṣiyyata li-l-qātil' (no will for a murderer) and notes the analogy (qiyās) to inheritance, which is also prohibited for a killer.
The ḥadīth is a ḥadīth āḥād (zannī al-thubūt) but its meaning (dalāla) is considered clear (qatʿī).
3
Note the exception and intra-Hanafi ikhtilāf
The matn says 'except with the permission of his heirs'. Abū Ḥanīfa and Muḥammad hold that the will becomes valid if the other heirs consent, because the prohibition is for their right. Abū Yūsuf holds it never becomes valid because the crime remains.
The muftā bihī is the view of Abū Ḥanīfa and Muḥammad, as preferred in al-Hidāyah.
4
Distinguish direct vs. indirect killing
The word 'directly' (mubāsharah) in the text excludes indirect causation (tasbīb), such as digging a well that causes someone to fall. A will for an indirect killer is valid.
This distinction is unique to the Hanafī school; other schools may treat indirect killing differently.
5
Compare with the Shāfiʿī position
Imam al-Shāfiʿī holds that a will for a murderer is valid regardless of heirs' consent, based on general permissibility. The Hanafī response is that the killer should not benefit from his crime.
The thamarah al-khilāf (practical consequence) is clear: under Shāfiʿī law, the will always stands; under Hanafī, it requires heirs' consent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقاتله مباشرة إلا بإجازة ورثته. [3] قوله: وقاتله؛ عطف على: وارثه؛ أي لا تصح الوصية للقاتل عامداً كان أو خاطئاً بعد أن كان مباشراً بقوله: (لا وصية للقاتل)؛ ولأنه استعجل ما آجره الله تعالى فيحرم الوصية كما تحرم الميراث إلا بإجازة ورثته؛ أي لو أجازتها الورثة جاز عند الطرفين. وقال أبو يوسف: لا تجوز؛ لأنّ جنايته باقية والامتناع لأجلها، ولهما أن الامتناع بحق الورثة؛ لأن نفع بطلانها يعود إليهم كنفع بطلان الميراث؛ ولأنهم لا يرضونها للقاتل، كما لا يرضونها لأحدهم، كما في «الهداية». قوله: مباشرة؛ احتراز عن القتل تسبيباً: كحفر البئر، وعند الشافعي تجوز الوصية للقاتل، وعلى هذا الخلاف إذا أوصى لرجل ثم إنه قتل الموصى.
And his direct murderer except with the permission of his heirs. [3] His saying: 'And his murderer': conjunct to 'his heir'; meaning, a will is not valid for the murderer, whether intentional or mistaken, provided he was direct, due to his saying: 'No will for a murderer'; and because he hastened what Allah delayed, so a will is prohibited just as inheritance is prohibited, except with the permission of his heirs; i.e., if the heirs permit it, it is permissible according to both sides. Abu Yusuf said: It is not permissible, because his crime remains and the prohibition is due to it, while for them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) the prohibition is due to the right of the heirs, because the benefit of its invalidation returns to them like the benefit of invalidating inheritance, and because they would not consent to it for the murderer just as they would not consent to it for one of them, as in Al-Hidayah. His saying: 'directly' is a qualification to exclude killing by cause, like digging a well, and according to al-Shafi'i a will for the murderer is valid, and on this difference if one makes a will for a man then the man kills the testator.
Source pages p. 583

Commentary
This passage addresses the prohibition of a will in favor of a direct murderer. The matn states that a will for a direct killer is invalid unless the other heirs consent. The commentary cites the hadith 'لا وصية للقاتل'. It explains the ruling applies whether the killing is intentional or accidental, as long as it is direct (مباشرة). There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say if the heirs consent, the will becomes valid, because the prohibition is for their right. Abu Yusuf says it never becomes valid because the crime remains. The text also notes the Shafi'i position, which allows a will for the killer entirely. The clarification 'مباشرة' excludes indirect killing (تسبيب).

Key Terms
مباشرة (mubasharah): Direct killing, as opposed to killing by cause (تسبيب).
تسبيب (tasbib): Indirect causation of death, e.g., digging a well that causes someone to fall.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Never
B) If the killer repents
C) If the other heirs consent after death
D) If the killing was accidental

Key Takeaway
A will for a direct murderer is invalid but can be revived by heirs' consent, distinguishing direct from indirect killing.

The Invalidity of a Minor's Will in Hanafi Fiqh
States that a minor's will is not valid in Hanafi school

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on a minor's will and explain the basis for the disagreement with the Shafi'i school.

Core Idea
A minor's will is void because a will is a donation, and a minor lacks legal capacity to donate.

This passage clarifies a key inter-madhhab dispute. The Hanafi school holds that a minor's will is invalid because it is a donation (تبرع), and a minor is not legally capable of donation. The Shafi'i school allows it under certain conditions. Understanding this disagreement helps grasp how capacity (أهلية) governs testamentary acts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a will made by a minor. According to the Hanafi school, the ruling is batil (void).
2
Identify the basis for the Hanafi ruling
The passage gives two reasons: a will is a donation (تبرع), and a minor's statement is not legally binding (غير ملزم).
The Hanafi position is rooted in the principle that a minor lacks أهلية الأداء (capacity to perform legal acts).
3
Contrast with the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i permits a minor's will if the minor is discerning (mumayyiz), considering that a discerning minor may have sufficient capacity.
The Shafi'i school focuses on the minor's actual comprehension rather than a categorical bar.
4
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
In Hanafi practice, the will is void and cannot be executed. In Shafi'i practice, the will may be enforced if the minor is discerning.
The difference affects inheritance planning and estate distribution in communities that follow these schools.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(ولا من صبي)، هذا عندنا، وعند الشَّافعي تجوز. [1] قوله: ولا من صبي؛ لأنها تبرع، والصبي ليس من أهله؛ ولأن قوله غيرُ ملزم، وفي تصحيح وصيته قول بإلزام قوله.
(And not from a minor), this with us, and according to al-Shafi'i it is permissible. [1] His saying: 'And not from a minor': because it is a donation, and a minor is not qualified for it, and because his statement is not binding, and in validating his will is a statement compelling his statement.
Source pages p. 584

Commentary
This passage presents a clear disagreement between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools on the validity of a minor's will. The Hanafi position is that a minor's will is invalid because a will is a form of donation (تبرع), and a minor lacks the legal capacity to make donations. Additionally, a minor's words are not legally binding. The Shafi'i school, however, permits a minor's will under certain conditions, possibly if the minor is discerning (mumayyiz). The Hanafi position is based on the principle that a will is a transaction requiring full legal capacity.

Key Terms
صبي (sabi): A minor who has not reached puberty or legal majority.
تبرع (tabarru'): Donation or voluntary transfer of property without exchange.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A minor's will is void per Hanafi law because a will is a donation requiring full legal capacity, which a minor does not possess.

Validity of a Mukatab's Will: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on a mukatab's will

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on a mukatab's will according to Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, explain the legal reasoning (ta'lil) for each position, and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
A mukatab's will is invalid according to Abu Hanifa because his property cannot be donated; Sahibayn hold it valid based on ownership.

In this lesson, we examine an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the validity of a will made by a mukatab—a slave under contract of manumission. While the mukatab has some ownership rights, Abu Hanifa restricts those rights when it comes to donation (تبرع), including a will. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) take a broader view of ownership. Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens your grasp of how property rights and capacity to donate interact in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and the two positions
Read the passage to determine the fiqh issue (validity of a mukatab's will) and note that Abu Hanifa says it is invalid, while the Sahibayn say it is valid.
2
Analyze Abu Hanifa's reasoning
The passage states: 'because his property does not accept donation, and a will is a donation.' Understand that the key legal cause (illah) is the lack of full ownership for donations.
This is a ta'lil (reasoning) based on the nature of the mukatab's property.
3
Analyze the Sahibayn's counter-reasoning
Though not fully detailed in the passage, the Sahibayn consider the mukatab's ownership rights sufficient to make a will. Infer that they see the property as freely owned once the contract is signed.
The Sahibayn equate the mukatab's ownership to that of a free person for wills.
4
Note the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Abu Hanifa, the will is void, and the mukatab's estate passes according to inheritance rules. Under the Sahibayn, the will is executed and the recipient inherits.
This affects distribution of the mukatab's assets after death.
5
Identify the mufta bihi (operative opinion)
The passage does not give a tarjih. In later Hanafi practice, Abu Hanifa's view is often preferred as a precaution (ihtiyat), but the ikhtilaf remains valid.
Some later texts may follow the Sahibayn; check the context of fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومكاتب وإن ترك وفاء. [2] قوله: ومكاتب... الخ؛ أي لا تصح وصيّة المكاتب، وإن ترك وفاء؛ لأنّ ماله لا يقبل التبرع، والوصية تبرع، وقيل: هذا قول أبي حنيفة له، وعندهما: تصح؛ وتمامه في «الهداية».
And a mukatab even if he leaves assets sufficient to pay his kitabah. [2] His saying: 'And a mukatab... etc.': That is, a will of a mukatab is not valid, even if he leaves assets sufficient to pay, because his property does not accept donation, and a will is a donation, and it is said: This is the view of Abu Hanifa alone, and according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) it is valid; and its completion is in Al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 584

Commentary
This passage discusses the validity of a will made by a mukatab (a slave who has entered into a contract of manumission upon payment). According to Abu Hanifa, his will is invalid even if he has enough assets to pay his kitabah, because his property is not fully owned by him for the purpose of donation (تبرع). The will is considered a donation, and a mukatab cannot make donations since his property is tied to the contract. However, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that his will is valid because he has ownership rights. This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has made a contract with his master to earn and pay for his freedom in installments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A mukatab's will is invalid according to Abu Hanifa due to restricted ownership, but valid according to the Sahibayn.

Debt Takes Priority Over Will: The Principle of Al-Ahamm
Explains priority of debt repayment over will execution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the priority rule of debt over will, explain the reasoning based on obligation vs donation, and apply the rule to scenarios where debts fully or partially cover the estate.

Core Idea
Debt repayment is an obligation; will is a donation — obligation always precedes donation.

In Islamic inheritance, the estate of a deceased must first settle any outstanding debts before any bequests are executed. This lesson explores the rationale behind this priority: because debt is a compulsory obligation and a will is a voluntary act, the more important need takes precedence. We will also examine the nuance when debts do not fully consume the estate and the exception of creditor forgiveness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two claims on the estate
The estate is subject to two types of claims: debts owed by the deceased, and bequests made in the will. Recognize that both are rights of others, but different in nature.
2
Compare their legal natures
Analyze the nature of each: debt is an obligation (farḍ/waajib) because it must be paid; a will is a donation (tabarru') which is only recommended. The Shari'ah gives priority to obligations over voluntary acts.
The text says: 'li-annahu farḍun wa al-wasiyyatu tabarru'.
3
Apply the principle of precedence (al-ahamm)
The general rule is to begin with the most important (al-ahamm fa al-ahamm). Since debt is more important, it is executed first. Consider the exception: if the creditors forgive the debt, then the will can be executed within its legal limit (one-third of the remaining property).
Exception: 'illa an yubari’a al-ghuramā'.
4
Distinguish between full and partial debt coverage
If the debt encompasses all property, nothing remains for the will unless creditors forgive. If the debt does not encompass, the will is executed from one-third of the remaining property after debt payment.
Text: 'hādhā fī al-dayn al-muḥīṭ bi-mālihī; ammā idhā lam yakun muḥīṭan, fa al-wasiyyatu tajrī fī thuluthi baqiyyati mālihī.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقدم الدين عليها. [3] قوله: وقدم الدين عليها؛ أي على الوصيّة؛ لأنه أهم الحاجتين، فلأنه فرض والوصية تبرع، وأبداً يبدأ بالأهم فالأهم إلا أن يبرئة الغرماء؛ لأنه لم يبق الدين فتنفذ الوصية على الحد المشروع، وهذا في الدين المحيط بماله، أما إذا لم يكن محيط فالوصية تجري في ثلث بقية ماله.
And debt is given precedence over it. [3] His saying: 'And debt is given precedence over it': i.e., over the will; because it is the more important of the two needs, because it is an obligation and the will is a donation, and always the most important is started with, unless the creditors release him; because then there is no debt, so the will is executed within the legal limit, and this is when debt encompasses his property, but if it does not encompass, then the will applies to one-third of the remaining property.
Source pages p. 584

Commentary
This passage explains the priority of debt over a will (وصية). Debts are compulsory (فرض) while a will is voluntary (تبرع), so debt repayment takes precedence. If the deceased's debts consume all his property, the will is not executed unless the creditors forgive the debts. If the debts do not cover all the property, the will applies to one-third of the remaining assets after debt payment. This is a ruling on the order of distribution from the estate.

Key Terms
الدين (al-dayn): Debts owed by the deceased, which must be settled before the will.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the priority between debt repayment and execution of a will?
A) Will first, then debts
B) Debts first, then will
C) Both simultaneously
D) It depends on the testator's wish

Key Takeaway
Debt repayment is obligatory and must be executed before any will, regardless of the testator's wishes, unless creditors forgive the debt.

The Timing of Acceptance in Wills
Ruling statement with ta'lil

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that acceptance of a will must occur after the testator's death and explain the legal reasoning (ta'lil) based on the will's attachment to death.

Core Idea
Acceptance of a will is only valid after death — the will's legal effect begins posthumously.

This passage addresses a fundamental rule of wills (wasiyyah) in Hanafi fiqh: the beneficiary's acceptance must occur after the testator's death. Why? Because a will is a transfer of ownership tied to death, so its ruling only arises after death. Let's explore the reasoning and analogy to contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
Extract from the text the precise rule about when a will can be accepted. The text states: 'And it is accepted after his death.'
The ruling is a condition for validity.
2
Understand the Ta'lil (Legal Reasoning)
The text gives the reason: 'Because the time for establishing its ruling is after death due to its attachment to it.' A will is a transfer of ownership (tamlik) attached to death (mudaf ila al-mawt), so its legal effect only arises after death.
Attachment (ta'alluq) means the will is contingent on death.
3
Consider the Analogy
The text analogizes to a contract: 'just as it is not considered before a contract.' Before a contract is concluded, any acceptance is irrelevant. Similarly, before death, the will is not operative.
This reinforces that the will is not a completed transaction during life.
4
Apply the Rule
Conclude what happens if acceptance occurs during the testator's life: it is not legally considered (la yu'tabar). The beneficiary must renew acceptance after death.
This ensures the will reflects the beneficiary's final decision after the testator's death.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتقبل بعد موته. [4] قوله: وتقبل ... الخ؛ لأنّ آوان ثبوتِ حكمِهِ بعد الموتِ لتعلقه : به، فلا يعتبر قبله، كما لا يعتبر قبل العقد.
And it is accepted after his death. [4] His saying: 'And it is accepted ... etc.': Because the time for establishing its ruling is after death due to its attachment to it, so it is not considered before, just as it is not considered before a contract.
Source pages p. 584

Commentary
This passage states that the acceptance of a will (وصية) by the beneficiary is only valid after the testator's death. Since a will is a transfer of ownership that is attached to death (تمليك مضاف إلى الموت), its legal effect arises posthumously. Therefore, any acceptance or rejection during the testator's lifetime is not legally recognized. This is analogous to a contract that cannot take effect before it is concluded.

Key Terms
قبول (qabul): Acceptance of the will by the beneficiary, which is only valid after the testator's death.
وصية (wasiyyah): A will or bequest, a transfer of ownership attached to death.
تمليك مضاف إلى الموت (tamlik mudaf ila al-mawt): A transfer of ownership that is contingent upon death; the basic nature of a will.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) At any time
D) Only if in writing

Key Takeaway
Acceptance of a will must occur after the testator's death because the will's legal effect is attached to death.

6 min
Rejection of Will During Lifetime is Void
Explains that rejection of a will before the testator's death is ineffective

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on rejection of a will during the testator's lifetime and explain why it is void.

Core Idea
A will is a transfer attached to death; rejection during life has no legal effect.

This lesson examines a key rule in Hanafi wasiyyah (will) law: any acceptance or rejection of a will performed before the testator's death is legally void. The reason is that a will is a contingent transfer that only takes effect upon death. Understanding this principle prevents confusion about when a beneficiary's actions become binding.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal ruling
What act is being ruled upon (acceptance and rejection of a will during life)? What is the ruling (void/batil)?
The text states 'بطل قبولها وردها في حياته' — its acceptance and rejection during his life are void.
2
Understand the rationale
Why is it void? Because a will is a transfer contingent upon death (تمليك متعلق بالموت). Actions before death are not relevant.
The will attaches to the property as it exists at death, not at the time of the will.
3
Apply the example
The text gives the example of bequeathing one-third of sheep: the beneficiary gets what exists at death, not at the time of will. Consider how this illustrates the principle.
The example shows that the object of the will is determined at death.
4
Note the practical consequence
If a beneficiary rejects during life, he can still accept after death; the earlier rejection is ignored.
This ensures that the will is not altered by premature actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبطل قبولها وردها في حياته. [5] قوله: وبطل قبولها وردها... الخ؛ حتى لو ردّها في حياته ثم قبلها بعد مماته تصح؛ لأنّ الوصية تمليك متعلّق بالموت ألا ترى أنه لو أوصى بثلث [غنمه] استحق الموصى له بثلث ما يوجد في ملك الموصي عند موته لا بثلث ما يوجد عند الوصية. كذا في الجلبي ناقلا عن «المعراجية».
And its acceptance and rejection during his life are void. [5] His saying: 'And its acceptance and rejection void... etc.': So much so that if he rejects it during his life and then accepts it after his death, it is valid; because a will is a transfer attached to death, do you not see that if he bequeaths one-third of [his sheep?], the beneficiary becomes entitled to one-third of what is found in the testator's ownership at his death, not one-third of what is found at the time of the will. Thus in al-Jalabi quoting from al-Mi'rajiyyah.
Source pages p. 584

Commentary
This passage explains that both acceptance and rejection of a will performed during the testator's lifetime are legally void. The reason is that a will is a transfer of ownership that is contingent upon death (تمليك متعلّق بالموت). The example given: if someone bequeaths a third of his sheep, the beneficiary is entitled to a third of what the testator owns at the time of death, not at the time of making the will. Therefore, if the beneficiary rejects the will during the testator's life, he can still accept it after death, and the rejection is ignored.

Key Terms
رد (radd): Rejection of the will by the beneficiary, which is ineffective if done during the testator's life.
تمليك متعلق بالموت (tamlik muta'allaq bil-mawt): A transfer of ownership that is contingent upon death; the defining characteristic of a will.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The will becomes void permanently
B) The rejection is void; he can still accept after death
C) The will becomes void but can be revived
D) The rejection is valid if witnessed

Key Takeaway
Rejection or acceptance of a will before death has no legal effect; only actions after death are valid.

Invalidity of a Will from a Minor and a Mukatab
Ruling on wills from minors and mukatabs with inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to: (1) state the Hanafi ruling that a minor's will is invalid and a mukatab's will is invalid according to Abu Hanifa; (2) explain the reasoning based on lack of legal capacity for donation and debt precedence; and (3) compare the Shafi'i view on the minor's will and the Sahibayn's view on the mukatab's will.

Core Idea
A minor lacks capacity for donation; a mukatab's property is encumbered by debt — both make their wills invalid.

In this passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya, we examine two categories of people whose wills are invalid in Hanafi law: a minor (sabi) and a mukatab (a slave under contract of kitabah). The matn states the rule, and the commentary explains the rationale: a will is a donation (tabarru'), and only those with full legal capacity for donation can make a valid will. Additionally, debt takes precedence over a will. We will also explore the disagreements with al-Shafi'i regarding the minor and the internal Hanafi disagreement regarding the mukatab.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two persons
List the two persons from whom a will is invalid according to the Hanafi school: a minor (sabi) and a mukatab (a slave with a kitabah contract).
The matn also states that debt is given precedence over the will.
2
Understand the reasoning for the minor
Explain why a minor's will is invalid: a will is a donation (tabarru'), and a minor lacks legal capacity (ahliyyah) for donation; his words are not binding.
This is the Hanafi position; al-Shafi'i disagrees.
3
Analyze the mukatab's case
The mukatab's will is invalid according to Abu Hanifa even if he has enough property to pay his debt, because his property is not free for donation while the debt exists. However, the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) consider it valid.
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is Abu Hanifa's view.
4
Apply the rule of debt precedence
Debt (dayn) takes precedence over the will because debt is a binding obligation (fard) while a will is a donation (tabarru'). If debt covers all assets, the will is ineffective unless creditors forgive.
This applies universally: debt is paid first, then one-third of the remainder is for bequests.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا من صبي، ومكاتب وإن ترك وفاء وقدمَ الدِّين عليها.
(ولا من صبي) ، هذا عندنا ، وعند الشَّافعي تجوز.
[١] قوله : ولا من صبي ؛ لأنها تبرع ، والصبي ليس من أهله ؛ ولأن قوله غيرُ ملزم، وفي تصحيح وصيته قول بإلزام قوله.
[٢] قوله : ومكاتب... الخ ؛ أي لا تصح وصيّة المكاتب، وإن ترك وفاء ؛ لأنّ ماله لا يقبل التبرع، والوصية تبرع ، وقيل : هذا قول أبي حنيفة له، وعندهما: تصح ؛ وتمامه في «الهداية».
[٣] قوله : وقدم الدين عليها ؛ أي على الوصيّة ؛ لأنه أهم الحاجتين ، فلأنه فرض والوصية تبرع ، وأبداً يبدأ بالأهم فالأهم إلا أن يبرئة الغرماء ؛ لأنه لم يبق الدين فتنفذ الوصية على الحد المشروع، وهذا في الدين المحيط بماله، أما إذا لم يكن محيط فالوصية تجري في ثلث بقية ماله.
And not from a minor, nor from a mukatab even if he leaves enough (to pay debt) and debt is given precedence over it. (The phrase 'not from a minor'), this is according to us, and according to al-Shafi'i it is permissible. He said: 'and not from a minor' because it is a donation, and a minor is not eligible for it; and because his word is not binding, and in validating his will there is a view that his word is binding. He said: 'and from a mukatab... etc.' meaning the will of a mukatab is not valid even if he leaves enough (to pay debt) because his property does not permit donation, and a will is a donation. It is said: this is the view of Abu Hanifa, but according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) it is valid; and its completion is in al-Hidayah. He said: 'and debt is given precedence over it' i.e. over the will because it is the more important of the two needs, because it is an obligation and the will is a donation, and always one begins with the most important then the next, unless the creditors release him because the debt remains; so the will is executed within the prescribed limit. This is when debt encompasses his wealth; but if it does not encompass, then the will applies to one third of the remainder of his wealth.
Source pages p. 584

Commentary
This passage from the matn establishes the invalidity of a will from two categories of people: a minor (sabi) and a mukatab (a slave who has contracted to purchase his freedom). The commentary expands on each. For the minor, the Hanafi position is that his will is invalid because a will is a donation (tabarru') and a minor lacks legal capacity for donation; his words are not binding. Al-Shafi'i disagrees and considers it valid. For the mukatab, Abu Hanifa holds that his will is invalid even if he has property sufficient to pay off his debt, because his wealth is not free for donation while he is still in debt. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) differ and consider it valid, and this is mentioned in al-Hidayah. The third point clarifies that debt (dayn) takes precedence over a bequest (wasiyyah) because debt is a binding obligation (fard) while a will is a donation (tabarru') and is therefore secondary. If debt encompasses all of the deceased's wealth, the will is ineffective unless creditors forgive the debt. If debt is less than the total wealth, the will applies to one-third of the remaining assets after debt.

Key Terms
صبي (sabi): A minor who has not reached puberty; legally incapable of making a donation.
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has a contract of kitabah to purchase his freedom by paying installments.
وصية (wasiyyah): A bequest or will; a donation made to take effect after death.
دين (dayn): Debt owed by the deceased; takes precedence over wills as a binding obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on a will made by a mukatab (slave under kitabah) who has sufficient property to pay his debt?
A) It is valid according to all Hanafi imams.
B) It is invalid according to Abu Hanifa, but valid according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.
C) It is valid only if the creditors consent.
D) It is invalid because a mukatab cannot own property.

Key Takeaway
A valid will requires full legal capacity for donation; a minor and a mukatab (per Abu Hanifa) lack this capacity, and debt always takes precedence over bequests.
