Acceptance of a Bequest After Death
Rulings on acceptance after death, revocation, and exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi requirement for acceptance of a bequest after death, identify the exception for the legatee's death, and apply the distinction between bequest and inheritance.

Core Idea
A bequest needs the legatee's acceptance after death; rejection during life is void.

Acceptance of a will is not like inheriting a gift. The testator’s death triggers the bequest, but the legatee must formally or implicitly agree to take it. The Hanafi school carefully distinguishes this from inheritance, where ownership transfers automatically. Let's examine the rules and the one exception allowed by istihsan.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main ruling
Locate the statement that a bequest is accepted after the testator's death. Note that acceptance or rejection during the testator's lifetime is null.
The time of acceptance is fixed after death because the bequest takes effect at death.
2
Find the exception
Identify the one case where explicit acceptance is not required: when the legatee dies before accepting. The bequest then passes to the legatee's heirs by istihsan.
Istihsan overrides qiyas here; the analogy to a sale with an option for the buyer is used.
3
Analyze the proof (refutation of Zufar)
Zufar argued acceptance is unnecessary, comparing to inheritance. The Hanafi response is that a bequest establishes new ownership for a non-heir, requiring consent, while inheritance is automatic for heirs.
The distinction: inheritance imposes a right; bequest creates a new right.
4
Understand the role of istihsan
Recognize that istihsan is used to validate the exception: the testator's side is complete at death, so if the legatee dies without rejecting, it enters his ownership.
This prevents the bequest from being invalidated by the legatee's death.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Given a scenario where a legatee rejects during the testator's life but accepts after death, determine the ruling.
The rejection during life is void; acceptance after death is valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتقبل بعد موته، وبطل قبولها وردها في حياته، وبه.
[٤] قوله : وتقبل ... الخ؛ لأنّ آوان ثبوتِ حكمِهِ بعد الموتِ لتعلقه : به ، فلا يعتبر قبل العقد.
قوله : وبطل قبولها وردّها... الخ؛ حتى لو ردّها في حياته ثم قبلها بعد مماته تصح ؛ لأنّ الوصية تمليك متعلّق بالموت ألا ترى أنه لو أوصى بثلث [غنمه] استحق الموصى له بثلث ما يوجد في ملك الموصي عند موته لا بثلث ما يوجد عند الوصية. كذا في الجلبي ناقلا عن «المعراجية».
أي بالقبول بعد الموت ، تملك إلا إذا مات موصيه ثمّ هو : أي الموصى له ، بلا قبول فهو لورثته ) : أي لورثة الموصى له.
[١] قوله : بالقبول ؛ أي لا يملك الوصية بلا قبول صريح، إلا في مسألة واحدة، وهي ما إذا ماتت... الخ، فإنّها تملك فيه القبول ضمني ؛ لأن موت الموصى له بلا ردّ كقبوله دلالة. كذا في «الجلبي» ناقلاً عن «البيانية».
[٢] قوله : تملك ؛ أي الموصى به يملك القبول خلافاً لزفر ، وهو يقيس الوصية على الميراث، وحكم الوراثة تثبت من غير قبول ، فكذا هذا، ولنا : إنّ الوصية إثباتُ ملك جديد لغيره، وإثبات الملكِ لغيره لا يكون إلا بالقبول، والميراث ليس كذلك، بل خلافه فافترقا كما لا يخفى على المتأمل.
[٣]قوله : إلا إذا ..الخ ؛ يعني الموصي به يملك بلا قبول في مسألة واحدة: وهي أن يموت الوصي ثمّ الموصى له قبل القبول، فيدخلُ الموصى به في ملك ورثة الموصى له استحساناً، والقياس أن تبطل الوصيّة، لما بينا من أنّ الوصية إثبات ملك جديد... الخ. وجه الاستحسان : إن الوصيّة من جانب الموصى به قد تمت بموته تماماً لا يلحقه الفسخ من جانبه، وإنّما توقفت من جانب الموصى له إن شاء ردّه، وإن شاء أجاز، فإذا مات دخل في ملكه كما في البيع المشروط بالخيار للمشتري إذا مات. هذا خلاصه ما في «الهداية».
And it is accepted after his death, and its acceptance and rejection become void during his lifetime, and by it. He said: 'and it is accepted... etc.' because the time of its ruling is after death because it is connected to it, so it is not considered before the contract. He said: 'and its acceptance and rejection become void... etc.' such that if he rejects it during his lifetime and then accepts it after his death, it is valid; because a bequest is a transfer of ownership connected to death. Do you not see that if he bequeaths one-third of his sheep, the legatee is entitled to one-third of what is found in the testator's possession at the time of his death, not one-third of what was found at the time of the bequest? Thus in al-Jalabi quoting from al-Mi'rajiyyah. That is, by acceptance after death, it is owned, except when his testator dies, then he – i.e. the legatee – without acceptance, it goes to his heirs, i.e. the heirs of the legatee. He said: 'by acceptance'; i.e. the bequest is not owned without explicit acceptance, except in one case, which is when she dies... etc., then implicit acceptance is considered, because the death of the legatee without rejection is like acceptance by indication. Thus in al-Jalabi quoting from al-Bayaniyyah. He said: 'is owned'; i.e. the legatee owns the bequest by acceptance, contrary to Zufar, who analogizes a bequest to inheritance, and the ruling of inheritance is established without acceptance, so here too. But we say: a bequest is establishing new ownership for another, and establishing ownership for another is only by acceptance, and inheritance is not like that; rather it is the opposite, so they differ, as is not hidden from the thoughtful person. He said: 'except when... etc.'; meaning the bequest is owned without acceptance in one case: when the testator dies and then the legatee dies before acceptance, then the bequest enters the ownership of the legatee's heirs by istihsan, and by qiyas the bequest would be invalid, because we have explained that a bequest is establishing new ownership... etc. The reason for istihsan is that the bequest from the testator's side is completed by his death, not subject to rescission from his side, but it is contingent from the legatee's side; if he wishes he rejects, if he wishes he accepts. When he dies, it enters his ownership, like in a sale with a condition of option for the buyer if he dies. This is the summary from al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 584

Commentary
This passage deals with the acceptance of a will. The matn states that a bequest is accepted after the testator's death, and any acceptance or rejection during his lifetime is void. The commentary explains the reasoning: the bequest is a transfer of ownership that takes effect at death, so actions before death are irrelevant. If the testator rejects the will during his lifetime but later accepts after death, the acceptance is valid because the will is only effective after death. An example is given: if someone bequeaths one-third of his sheep, the legatee is entitled to one-third of the sheep owned at death, not at the time of the will. Then the text discusses whether acceptance is always required. It states that generally, explicit acceptance is necessary; without it, the bequest is not owned. However, there is one exception: if the legatee dies without having explicitly accepted, the bequest passes to his heirs (implicit acceptance). This exception is based on istihsan, as the bequest from the testator is complete at his death; the legatee's right is contingent, and his death without rejection is considered acceptance. This is analogized to a sale with a condition of option for the buyer: if the buyer dies without rejecting, the sale becomes effective. The passage also refutes Zufar, who analogized a bequest to inheritance (where acceptance is not needed). The Hanafi position is that a bequest creates new ownership for a non-heir, which requires acceptance, unlike inheritance which is automatic.

Key Terms
قبول (qabul): The explicit or implicit acceptance of a bequest by the legatee after the testator's death.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference used to rule that if the legatee dies without rejection, the bequest enters his ownership by implied acceptance.
تمليك (tamlik): Transfer of ownership; a bequest is a tamlik that takes effect after death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under Hanafi law, if a testator bequeaths a property, and the legatee rejects the bequest during the testator's lifetime but accepts after the testator's death, what is the ruling?
A) The rejection is binding, and the bequest becomes void.
B) The rejection is ignored, and acceptance after death is valid.
C) The bequest goes to the testator's heirs.
D) The testator must confirm the bequest again.

Key Takeaway
A bequest requires the legatee's acceptance after the testator's death, except if the legatee dies before accepting, then it passes to his heirs by istihsan.

Revocation of a Will by the Testator
Ruling on the testator's right to revoke the will

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the testator's right to revoke a will and identify the methods of revocation in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
A will is a non-binding donation; the testator may revoke it anytime before death.

This short passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah confirms a key principle: the testator retains full control over a will during his lifetime. Since a will is a donation that only takes effect after death, it is not binding beforehand. The Hanafi school thus permits revocation by either explicit words or any action that removes the bequeathed property from the testator's ownership.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal act
Recognize that the passage is about the testator's act of revoking a will (al-ruju' 'an al-wasiyyah).
2
State the ruling
The ruling (hukm) is that revocation is permissible (ja'iz / mubah) at any time before death.
The text uses 'yajuz' (permissible).
3
Analyze the reasoning
The rationale is that a will is a donation (tabarru') that has not been completed; it becomes effective only after death. Therefore, it is revocable, like a gift that has not been delivered.
This is based on the principle of non-binding tabarru'.
4
List the methods of revocation
Revocation can be done by: (1) explicit words, e.g., 'I revoke my bequest to so-and-so'; (2) any action that removes the property from the testator's ownership, such as selling or giving it away.
The text references the chapter on usurpation for the principle of actions that cut off ownership.
5
Apply to scenarios
Distinguish between valid revocation (explicit words or action removing ownership) and invalid attempts (mere thoughts, ambiguous statements, or actions that do not affect ownership).
Only clear words or dispositive actions work.

Source Text

وله أن يرجع عنها بقول صريح ، أو فعل يقطعُ حق المالكِ عَمَّا غَصَبَ كما مر
[٤] قوله : وله... الخ ؛ يعني يجوز للموصي أن يرجع عن الوصية ؛ لأنه تبرع لم يتم، فجاز الرجوع عنه كالهبة بقول صريح، وهو يقول : رجعت عمّا أوصيت به لفلان أو بفعل يقطع حق المالك عمّا غصب.
And he may revoke it by explicit words or by an action that cuts off the owner's right, as mentioned in the chapter on usurpation.
He said: 'and he may... etc.' meaning it is permissible for the testator to revoke the will because it is a donation that has not been completed, so revocation is permissible, like a gift, by explicit words – he says: I revoke what I bequeathed to so-and-so – or by an action that cuts off the owner's right regarding what was usurped.
Source pages p. 585

Commentary
This short passage confirms the testator's right to revoke a will. The basis is that a will is a donation (tabarru') that becomes effective only after death. Since it is not yet completed during the testator's lifetime, he may revoke it, similar to a gift that has not been delivered. Revocation can be done explicitly by stating that he revokes the bequest, or implicitly through any action that removes the bequeathed property from his ownership, such as selling it or giving it away as a gift. The commentary references the earlier discussion in the chapter on usurpation (ghasb) where similar principles apply.

Key Terms
رجوع (ruju'): Revocation of a will by the testator during his lifetime.
تبرع (tabarru'): A voluntary donation; a will is a tabarru' that becomes effective after death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) A will cannot be revoked once made.

Key Takeaway
A will is not binding during the testator's lifetime; he may revoke it at any time by explicit words or any action that removes the bequeathed property from his ownership.

Ownership of a Bequest: The Role of Acceptance
Ruling on acceptance for bequest ownership with exception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general rule that acceptance is required for ownership of a bequest, identify the exception where the legatee's death before acceptance suffices, and explain the Hanafi istihsan reasoning.

Core Idea
A bequest is not owned without acceptance; only the legatee's death without rejection triggers ownership by istihsan.

In Hanafi law, a will is a deferred donation that becomes effective only after the testator's death. However, the legatee must actively accept the bequest to take ownership. This lesson unpacks the general requirement of acceptance, one exceptional case where acceptance is presumed, and the underlying legal reasoning that distinguishes a will from inheritance.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule
Identify the baseline: a bequest is only owned by the legatee after explicit or implicit acceptance following the testator's death.
Implicit acceptance includes actions indicating agreement.
2
Identify the exception
Find the one case where ownership occurs without acceptance: when the legatee dies before accepting, the bequest passes to the legatee's heirs.
This is by istihsan, overriding qiyas.
3
Understand the qiyas-istihsan conflict
Zufar analogizes wills to inheritance (no acceptance needed). The Hanafi majority distinguishes: a will creates new ownership, hence acceptance is needed, except when death prevents it.
Istihsan treats the legatee's unrejected death as implicit acceptance.
4
Apply to a scenario
Test your understanding: testator bequeaths a house to B. B dies before accepting. Who owns the house?
Ownership goes to B's heirs by istihsan.

Book of Wills / Its definition and rulings. It is owned except if his testator dies then it is without acceptance it is for his heirs, and he has the right to revoke it by explicit statement, or an action that cuts off the right of the owner from what was usurped as previously. Meaning by acceptance after death. It is owned except if his testator dies then it: i.e., the legatee, without acceptance it is for his heirs: i.e., for the heirs of the legatee. And he has the right to revoke it by explicit statement, or an action that cuts off the right of the owner from what was usurped as previously passed. It has passed in the Book of Usurpation his statement: For if he usurps and changes, so its name changes and greater... His statement: by acceptance; meaning the will is not owned without explicit acceptance, except in one matter, which is if she dies... etc., then she owns it by implicit acceptance because the death of the legatee without rejection is like acceptance by indication. This is in al-Jalabi quoting from al-Bayaniyya. His statement: it is owned; meaning the bequeathed item is owned with acceptance contrary to Zufar. He analogizes the will to inheritance, and the ruling of inheritance is established without acceptance, so this too. And for us: the will is an establishment of new ownership for another, and establishment of ownership for another is not except by acceptance, and inheritance is not like that, rather the opposite, so they differ as is not hidden from the contemplator. His statement: except if... etc.; meaning the bequeathed item is owned without acceptance in one matter: that the testator dies then the legatee before acceptance, so the bequeathed item enters into the ownership of the heirs of the legatee by istihsan, and by qiyas the will becomes void, because we have explained that the will is an establishment of new ownership... etc. The face of istihsan: that the will from the side of the testator has been completed by his death completely, no rescission can affect it from his side, but it was suspended from the side of the legatee: if he wishes he rejects, and if he wishes he approves. So when he dies, it enters his ownership like in a sale with a condition of option for the buyer when he dies. This is the summary of what is in al-Hidayah. His statement: and he has... etc.; meaning it is permissible for the testator to revoke the will because it is a donation that has not been completed, so revocation is permissible from it like a gift, by explicit statement, i.e., he says: I have revoked what I bequeathed to so-and-so, or by an action that cuts off the right of the owner from what was usurped. Or he increases in the bequeathed item what prevents its delivery except with it: like mixing semolina with clarified butter, and building in a house he bequeathed, and a disposition that removes his ownership like sale and gift. Not by washing a garment he bequeathed, nor by denial of it, contrary to Abu Yusuf, for denial is revocation according to him. And the gift of a sick person and his will to one he marries after it becomes void. Meaning: a sick person gave a gift to a woman or bequeathed something to her, then he married her, then he died, the gift and will become void; because the will is an obligation after death, and at death she is an heir to him. As for the gift, even if it is immediate, it is like added to death because its ruling is settled at death. Do you not see that it becomes void as if he bequeathed that this sheep is for so-and-so then he slaughtered it, or by an increase in the bequeathed item that prevents delivery of the bequeathed item except with what was added, like mixing semolina with clarified butter, and building in a house he bequeathed, or by a disposition that removes his ownership, as if he sold or gave away the bequeathed item. His statement: not by washing a garment... etc.; meaning washing the bequeathed garment is not revocation because whoever wants to give a garment to someone else usually washes it, so this washing is a confirmation of the will, not a revocation. His statement: nor by denial of it; meaning revocation is not established by the testator's denial of the will; by him saying: I did not bequeath to so-and-so, or he said: I have not bequeathed to him. His statement: revocation according to him; because revocation is a negation in the present, and denial is a negation in the past and present, so it is even more appropriate to be revocation. But praise be to Allah, revocation is an affirmation in the past and negation in the present, and denial is negation in the past and present, so it is not revocation; and for this reason denial of marriage is not separation. This is in al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 585

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the Book of Wills from a Hanafi fiqh work. It begins by stating the general rule: a bequest is not owned by the legatee without acceptance after the testator's death. However, there is one exception: if the testator dies and then the legatee dies before accepting, the bequeathed item enters the ownership of the legatee's heirs by istihsan (juristic preference). The qiyas (analogy) would render the will void because the legatee hadn't accepted, but istihsan treats the deceased legatee's silence as implied acceptance, likening it to a sale with a condition of option. The passage then discusses the testator's right to revoke the will. Since a will is a gratuitous donation that is not yet completed (the testator can change his mind before death), revocation is valid. Revocation can be by explicit statement (e.g., 'I revoke my bequest to so-and-so') or by any action that effectively prevents the delivery of the bequeathed item. Examples of such actions include mixing the bequeathed food with another ingredient so that it cannot be separated without the additive, building on the bequeathed land, or selling or giving away the item. Importantly, washing a bequeathed garment does not constitute revocation, as this is normally done before giving a gift and indicates intention to deliver. Similarly, merely denying the will (saying 'I did not bequeath') is not considered revocation according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, though Abu Yusuf disagrees. The passage concludes with a special ruling: a gift or will made by a sick person to a woman whom he later marries becomes void upon his death, because at the time of death she becomes an heir (if the marriage is valid), and one cannot be both a legatee/giftee and an heir from the same estate. This ruling applies even if the gift was immediate, as its final effect is considered at death.

Key Terms
وصية (wasiyyah): A legal disposition of property effective after death, requiring acceptance after death.
قبول (qabul): Explicit or implicit acceptance of a bequest after the testator's death.
استحسان (istihsan): A method of juristic preference that overrides qiyas (analogy) in this case to treat death without rejection as acceptance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, when is a bequest owned without explicit acceptance?
A) When the testator dies and the legatee immediately claims it
B) When the testator dies and then the legatee dies before accepting
C) When the legatee is a minor
D) When the bequest is less than one-third of the estate

Key Takeaway
Acceptance is essential for bequest ownership except when the legatee dies before accepting; then istihsan grants ownership to his heirs.

Revocation of a Will: Methods and the Dispute on Denial
Ruling on revocation methods and the intra-Hanafi dispute on denial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the valid methods of revoking a will (explicit statement and certain actions), distinguish between actions that revoke and those that do not, and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether denial of a will constitutes revocation.

Core Idea
A will can be revoked explicitly or by actions preventing delivery, but denying it is not revocation according to the majority.

Since a will is a donation that has not yet been completed (the testator can change his mind before death), revocation is permissible. However, not every act of the testator automatically revokes it. This lesson examines the clear methods of revocation and the debated case of denial, where Abu Yusuf diverges from the majority.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basis for revocation
A will is an incomplete donation (تبرع لم يتم), so the testator may revoke it before death.
This is analogous to a gift that has not been delivered.
2
List explicit and implicit methods
Explicit: saying 'I revoke my bequest to X'. Implicit: any action that cuts off the testator's ability to deliver the item, like selling it, giving it away, or mixing it with something inseparable.
The text gives examples: mixing semolina with clarified butter, building on bequeathed land.
3
Distinguish non-revoking actions
Washing a bequeathed garment does not revoke; it indicates care for delivery. Denial (جحود) is also not revocation according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
Abu Yusuf considers denial as revocation because it is more comprehensive.
4
Analyze the dispute on denial
Abu Yusuf argues denial negates the will entirely. The majority distinguish: revocation affirms past and negates present; denial negates both.
This linguistic analysis is key to the Hanafi position.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the Book of Wills from a Hanafi fiqh work. It begins by stating the general rule: a bequest is not owned by the legatee without acceptance after the testator's death. However, there is one exception: if the testator dies and then the legatee dies before accepting, the bequeathed item enters the ownership of the legatee's heirs by istihsan (juristic preference). The qiyas (analogy) would render the will void because the legatee hadn't accepted, but istihsan treats the deceased legatee's silence as implied acceptance, likening it to a sale with a condition of option. The passage then discusses the testator's right to revoke the will. Since a will is a gratuitous donation that is not yet completed (the testator can change his mind before death), revocation is valid. Revocation can be by explicit statement (e.g., 'I revoke my bequest to so-and-so') or by any action that effectively prevents the delivery of the bequeathed item. Examples of such actions include mixing the bequeathed food with another ingredient so that it cannot be separated without the additive, building on the bequeathed land, or selling or giving away the item. Importantly, washing a bequeathed garment does not constitute revocation, as this is normally done before giving a gift and indicates intention to deliver. Similarly, merely denying the will (saying 'I did not bequeath') is not considered revocation according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, though Abu Yusuf disagrees. The passage concludes with a special ruling: a gift or will made by a sick person to a woman whom he later marries becomes void upon his death, because at the time of death she becomes an heir (if the marriage is valid), and one cannot be both a legatee/giftee and an heir from the same estate. This ruling applies even if the gift was immediate, as its final effect is considered at death.

Key Terms
رجوع (ruju'): The act of revoking a will, permissible because it is an incomplete donation.
جحود (juhud): Denial of having made a will, e.g., saying 'I never bequeathed anything to so-and-so'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Washing the bequeathed garment

Key Takeaway
Revocation of a will requires an explicit statement or a definitive action that prevents delivery; mere denial does not revoke it.

Does Denial of a Will Revoke It? The Hanafi Position
Intra-Hanafi dispute on verbal denial as revocation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish acts that revoke a will from those that do not, and explain why verbal denial is not revocation according to the Hanafi majority.

Core Idea
Denying a will is not revoking it — active cancellation requires an action, not a statement negating the past.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah (a commentary on al-Wiqayah) clarifies which actions constitute revocation of a will. It lists clear revocations (e.g., selling the bequeathed item) and then addresses a debated point: whether simply saying 'I did not make a will' counts as revocation. The Hanafi majority says no, but Abu Yusuf dissents. We will examine the reasoning, including a key qiyas (analogy) to marriage.

Guided Path
1
Identify actions that revoke a will
Read the passage to find the three actions listed as clear revocation: (1) adding something that prevents delivery without it; (2) building in the bequeathed house; (3) any transaction that removes ownership (sale, gift). These all show a change of intention.
These actions make the original bequest impossible or indicate transfer of ownership.
2
Note the action that does NOT revoke
Washing a bequeathed garment is not revocation. Why? Because it is normal preparation for giving — it confirms the will.
3
Understand the intra-Hanafi dispute on denial
The testator says 'I did not bequeath to so‑and‑so.' Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say this does NOT revoke. Abu Yusuf says it does. Notice the different definitions of revocation.
Abu Yusuf's view: revocation is present negation; denial is stronger (past+present), so it should be revocation.
4
Trace the qiyas (analogy) used
The Hanafi majority uses an analogy: denial of marriage is not a divorce (separation). Similarly, denial of a will is not a revocation. The legal reasoning (illa) is that revocation requires affirming the past and negating the present, whereas denial negates both.
This is a qiyas khafi (hidden analogy) because the common illa is not explicit in the text.
5
Determine the mufta bihi position
The relied‑upon position (mufta bihi) is that denial does not revoke. This is the view taken by al‑Hidayah and the present commentary.
Practical effect: If the testator denies and then dies, the bequest is still valid according to the majority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو يزيد في الموصى به ما يمنع تسليمه إلا به: كلت السويق بسمن، والبناء في دار أوصى بها، وتصرف يزيل ملكه: كالبيع والهبة، لا بغسل ثوب أوصى به، ولا بجحودها، خلافاً لأبي يوسف، فإن الجحود رجوع عنده. لا بغسل ثوب... الخ؛ أي غسل الثوب الموصى به لا يكون رجوعاً؛ لأن من أراد أن يعطي ثوباً غيره يغسله عادة، فكان هذا الغسل تقريراً للوصية لا رجوعاً. ولا بجحودها؛ أي لا يثبت الرجوع لجحود الموصي الوصية؛ بأن قال: لم أوص لفلان، أو قال: ما أوصيت له. رجوع عنده؛ لأن الرجوع نفي في الحال، والجحود نفي في الماضي والحال، فأولى أن يكون رجوعاً، والحمد لله أن الرجوع إثبات في الماضي ونفي في الحال، والجحود نفي في الماضي والحال، فلا يكون رجوعاً؛ ولهذا لا يكون جحود النكاح فرقة. كذا في «الهداية».
Or he adds to the bequeathed item something that prevents its delivery except with it: mixing sawiq with ghee, building in a bequeathed house, and a transaction that removes his ownership: like sale and gift. Not washing a bequeathed garment, nor denial of it, contrary to Abu Yusuf, for denial is a revocation according to him. 'Not washing a garment...' i.e., washing the bequeathed garment does not constitute a revocation; because one who intends to give a garment to another usually washes it, so this washing is a confirmation of the will, not a revocation. 'Nor denial of it' i.e., revocation is not established by the testator's denial of the will, such as saying: 'I did not bequeath to so-and-so' or 'I have not bequeathed to him.' 'Revocation according to him' because revocation is negation in the present, and denial is negation in the past and present, so it is more fitting to be a revocation; but praise be to God, revocation is affirmation in the past and negation in the present, while denial is negation in the past and present, so it is not a revocation; hence denial of marriage is not a separation. Thus in al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 586

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute about what actions revoke a will. The passage first lists actions that constitute clear revocation: (1) adding something to the bequeathed item that prevents delivery without it, e.g., mixing sawiq with ghee to make it a new mixture; (2) building a structure in a bequeathed house; (3) any transaction that removes ownership, such as sale or gift. These indicate a change of intention. Then it mentions actions that are NOT revocation: washing a bequeathed garment and verbally denying the will (juhud). The commentator explains that washing is normal preparation for giving, so it confirms the will. As for denial, the majority Hanafi view (Abu Hanifa) holds that it is not revocation. Abu Yusuf disagrees, arguing that since revocation is a present negation, denial (present and past negation) is even stronger, so it should be revocation. The text rejects this: revocation affirmatively cancels the past intention, while denial merely denies any past intention, which is different. The ruling aligns with the mainstream Hanafi position: denial does not revoke, just as denial of marriage is not a divorce.

Key Terms
جحود (juhūd): Verbal denial of having made a will.
رجوع (rujūʿ): Act of revoking a will, cancelling its effect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi majority, which of the following is NOT a valid revocation of a will?
A) Selling the bequeathed item
B) Building a structure on the bequeathed land
C) Washing the bequeathed garment
D) Mixing the bequeathed sawiq with ghee

Key Takeaway
Denial (juhud) of a will does not revoke it according to the relied-upon Hanafi position; revocation requires an act that changes the testator's ownership or delivery.

Invalidity of Gifts and Bequests When the Recipient Becomes an Heir
Rulings on gifts and wills to someone who becomes an heir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on gifts and wills made by a sick person to someone who later becomes an heir, and explain the reasoning based on tuhmat al-īthār and the status of a gift as analogous to a bequest.

Core Idea
A gift from a sick person is treated like a bequest; if the recipient becomes an heir before death, the disposition is invalid.

When a person in terminal illness makes a gift or bequest to someone who is not an heir at that time, but later becomes an heir (e.g., by marriage, conversion, or manumission), what happens? The Hanafi school holds that such dispositions are invalid. This lesson explores the legal reasoning and two specific cases: a gift/bequest to a woman then married, and to a son who was an unbeliever or slave then converts or is freed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Scenarios
Read the passage and identify the two main scenarios: (1) gift/bequest to a woman then marriage, and (2) acknowledgment/gift/bequest to an unbeliever or slave son then conversion/manumission.
Both involve the recipient becoming a legal heir before the sick person's death.
2
Determine the Legal Status at Time of Act and Death
For each scenario, note that at the time of the disposition, the recipient was not an heir. At death, they become an heir (wife, Muslim/free son).
This change triggers the invalidity.
3
Analyze the Underlying Principle
The passage states that a sick person's gift is like a suspended gift (mudafa ila al-mawt) because its effect is finalized at death. It is analogized to a bequest, and bequests to heirs are prohibited (hadith: 'No bequest to an heir').
This is based on tuhmat al-īthār – suspicion of preferring the recipient over rightful heirs.
4
Apply the Principle to Both Cases
In the first case, the woman becomes a wife (heir) upon marriage; the gift/bequest fails. In the second, the son becomes a Muslim/free (heir) upon conversion/manumission; the gift/bequest/acknowledgment fails.
The acknowledgment (iqrar) also fails because the son's sonship existed at the time, raising suspicion.
5
Note the Broader Implications
Recognize that any disposition from a sick person that eventually benefits an heir is invalid unless other heirs consent. This includes gifts, bequests, and acknowledgments.
This ruling applies only to dispositions made during marad al-mawt (death illness).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتبطل هبة المريض ووصيته لمن نكحها بعدها: أي وهب المريض لامرأة شيئاً، أو أوصى لها بشيء، ثم تزوجها ثم مات، تبطل الهبة والوصية؛ لأن الوصية إيجاب بعد الموت، وعند الموت هي وارثة له، وأما الهبة فهي وإن كانت منجزة فهي كالمضافة إلى الموت؛ لأن حكمها يتقرر عند الموت، ألا ترى أنها تبطل كما أوصى أن هذه الشاة لفلان ثم ذبحها، أو بزيادة في الموصي به يمنع تسليم الموصى به إلا بما زاد كلت السويق بسمن، والبناء في دار أوصى بها، أو بتصرف يزيل ملكه، كما باع أو وهب الشيء الموصى به. كإقراره ووصيته وهبته لابنه كافراً أو عبداً إن أسلم أو أعتق بعد ذلك: أي إن أقر المريض، أو أوصى، أو وهب لابنه الكافر ثم أسلم الابن قبل موت الأب بطل ذلك كله؛ أما الإقرار فلأن البنوة قائمة وقت الإقرار، فاعتبر في الإيراث تهمة الإيثار، وأما الهبة والوصية فلما مر، فكذا إن كان الابن عبداً أو مكاتبا فعتق لما بينا.
And the gift and will of a sick person to a woman he marries afterward becomes invalid: meaning if a sick person gives something to a woman or bequeaths something to her, then marries her, then dies, the gift and will become invalid. Because the will is an obligation after death, and at death she is an heir for him. As for the gift, even though it is immediate, it is like a suspended gift; because its effect is determined at death. Do you not see that it becomes invalid just as if he bequeathed that this sheep is for so-and-so then slaughtered it, or by adding to the bequeathed item something that prevents delivery of the bequeathed item except with the addition, like mixing sawiq with ghee, or building in a bequeathed house, or by a transaction that removes his ownership, such as selling or giving away the bequeathed item. Like his acknowledgment, will, and gift to his son who is an unbeliever or slave, if after that he becomes Muslim or free: meaning if a sick person acknowledges, bequeaths, or gives to his unbeliever son, then the son becomes Muslim before the father's death, all that becomes invalid. As for the acknowledgment, because sonship exists at the time of acknowledgment, so suspicion of preference (tuhmat al-ithar) is considered in causing inheritance. As for the gift and will, due to what has passed. Similarly, if the son is a slave or mukatab and then becomes free, for what we have mentioned.
Source pages p. 586

Commentary
This passage extends the principle that a gift or will from a person in marad al-mawt (death illness) to someone who later becomes an heir is invalid. Two cases are given: (1) A sick man gives or bequeaths to a woman, then marries her; upon his death, she is his heir (wife), so the gift/will fails because the law prohibits bequests to heirs (except with other heirs' permission) and gifts from a sick person are treated as bequests from one-third. (2) A sick person acknowledges (iqrar), bequeaths, or gives to his son who is an unbeliever or slave; if the son later becomes Muslim or free before the father's death, the disposition is invalid. The reason: at the time of the act, the son is not an heir (unbeliever/slave cannot inherit), but if he becomes a Muslim/free before death, he becomes an heir, so the act is tainted by the suspicion of preference (tuhmat al-ithar). The gift from a sick person is considered a bequest in effect, so it must be from one-third and cannot benefit an heir. The commentary explains that the gift's effect is determined at death, so it is like a suspended gift (mudafa ila al-mawt).

Key Terms
تهمة الإيثار (tuhmat al-īthār): Suspicion of preferring the recipient over rightful heirs, which invalidates dispositions from a sick person.
إقرار (iqrār): Acknowledgment of a debt or obligation; here, acknowledgment of a son's right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A sick man gives a gift to his son who is an unbeliever. Before the father dies, the son converts to Islam. What happens to the gift?
A) It remains valid because the gift was completed before conversion.
B) It becomes invalid because the son becomes an heir.
C) It is valid only up to one-third of the estate.
D) It is valid if the father gives permission.

Key Takeaway
A disposition from a sick person to someone who later becomes an heir is invalid because it is treated as a bequest, and bequests to heirs are prohibited under the suspicion of preference.

Gifts from Chronically Ill Persons and Priority of Multiple Bequests
Rulings on gifts from chronically ill and priority among multiple bequests.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a chronically ill person's gift is valid from all wealth and apply the rule that obligatory bequests take priority over voluntary ones.

Core Idea
A gift from a chronically ill person is valid from all wealth if the illness is prolonged and death not feared; else limited to one-third.

This passage from the Hanafi tradition addresses two practical issues in wills and gifts. First, it clarifies when a person suffering from a long-term illness may give away his entire wealth without restriction, distinguishing such chronic conditions from terminal illness (marad al-mawt). Second, it establishes a clear priority among multiple bequests: obligations owed to Allah (like missed zakah and hajj) are paid first, even if the testator specified a later order. Understanding these rulings is crucial for drafting valid wills and protecting the rights of heirs.

Guided Path
1
Separate the two rulings
Recognize that the passage contains two distinct legal issues: (1) the validity of gifts from chronically ill persons, and (2) the priority when multiple wills exceed the one-third limit. Handle each separately.
The first ruling concerns lifetime gifts; the second concerns bequests after death.
2
Identify the condition for full validity
For the first ruling, note that a gift from a chronically ill person (maq‘ad, maflūj, ashal, maslūl) is valid from all his wealth only if two conditions are met: the illness is prolonged and he does not fear death. If either condition fails, the gift is limited to one-third.
Fear of death is subjective; the Hanafi school looks at the person's own expectation.
3
Define the key terms
The commentator defines each illness: maq‘ad (confined due to disease), maflūj (paralyzed on one side), ashal (withered hand), maslūl (tuberculosis). These definitions help determine whether the condition is chronic or terminal.
These terms are not exhaustive; the principle applies to any similar chronic condition.
4
Apply the priority rule for bequests
When multiple bequests (wasiyyas) are made and the estate cannot cover them all, obligatory (farḍ) bequests such as missed zakah, hajj, fasting, and prayers take precedence over voluntary bequests, regardless of the order specified by the testator.
'Farḍ' here includes both farḍ and wajib obligations according to the Hanafi school.
5
Connect to the broader Hanafi principle
The ruling is based on the principle that a person suffering from a prolonged illness without fear of death is not in 'marad al-mawt' (death illness), so his gifts are treated as ordinary lifetime gifts. The priority rule reflects that debts owed to Allah are like financial debts and must be settled first.
This aligns with the maxim: 'al-diyūn tuqḍa min al-tarikat' (debts are paid from the estate before bequests).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصح هبة مقعد، ومفلوج، وأشلّ، ومسلول من كل ماله إن طال مدته ولم يخف موته، وإلا فمن ثلثه. وإن اجتمع الوصايا قدم الفرض وإن أخر. المقعد: من لا يقدر على القيام لداء في جسده كأن الداء قعده، وعند الأطباء: هو الزمن. والمفلوج: من ذهب نصفه وبطل عنه الحبس، ولا يقدر على الحركة. والأشل: من شلت يده؛ لأن الشلل بالشين المعجمة آفة في اليد. والمسلول: بالسين المهملة، هو الذي به مرض السل، وهو عبارة عن اجتماع المدة في الصدر ونفسها. قدم الفرض؛ أي قدمه الموصي أو أخره كالزكاة والحج والصوم والصلاة.
And valid is the gift of a confined person (maq‘ad), a paralyzed person (maflūj), one with a withered hand (ashall), and one with tuberculosis (maslūl) from all of his wealth if his illness is prolonged and he does not fear death; otherwise from one-third. And if multiple wills coincide, the obligatory (farḍ) is given precedence even if it is delayed. The maq‘ad is one who cannot stand due to a disease in his body, as if the disease has confined him; according to physicians, it is the chronic invalid. The maflūj is one whose half has gone and his confinement is void, and he cannot move. The ashal is one whose hand is withered; because shalal with mu‘jama (shin) is a defect in the hand. The maslūl with muhmala (sin) is one who has the disease of consumption (tuberculosis), which is the collection of pus in the chest and its breathing. 'He gives precedence to the farḍ' i.e., the testator gives it precedence or delays it, like zakah, hajj, fasting, and prayer.
Source pages p. 587

Commentary
This passage provides two distinct rulings. First, regarding gifts made by individuals suffering from chronic conditions that are not necessarily fatal (maq‘ad, maflūj, ashal, maslūl). If such a person's condition has lasted a long time and he does not fear imminent death, his gift is considered valid from all of his wealth (not limited to one-third) because he is not deemed to be in marad al-mawt (death illness). However, if his condition is short-term and he fears death, then the gift is treated as a bequest and is limited to one-third of his estate. The commentator defines each term to clarify the conditions. Second, when multiple wills (bequests) are made and the estate cannot cover them all, the obligatory (farḍ) bequest is given priority, even if the testator specified a later order. The commentator explains that 'farḍ' here includes wajibat like zakah, hajj, fasting, and prayer that may be bequeathed to be performed on behalf of the deceased (e.g., hiring someone to perform missed acts). These take precedence over voluntary bequests because they are debts owed to Allah.

Key Terms
مقعد (maq‘ad): Someone who cannot stand due to a disease; a chronic invalid.
الفرض (al-farḍ): Obligatory bequests like zakah, hajj, etc., that are debts upon the deceased.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When multiple wills are made, which type of bequest is given priority?
A) The one made first in time
B) The one for charity
C) The obligatory (farḍ) bequest
D) The bequest to a relative

Key Takeaway
A chronically ill person's gift is from all wealth if the illness is prolonged and death not feared; obligatory bequests always take priority over voluntary ones.

Bequest to an Infidel or Slave Son: Ruling and Reasoning
Ruling on bequest to an heir with conditional status change

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a deathbed bequest to a son who is an infidel or slave, and explain why the son's conversion or freedom before death does not validate the disposition.

Core Idea
A bequest to an heir remains invalid even if the heir ceases to be an heir before death, because the defect is at the time of disposition.

In Hanafi law, a bequest to an heir is prohibited without the consent of other heirs, due to the suspicion of partiality (tuhmat al-īthār). This lesson examines a specific case: a father makes a bequest to his son who is an infidel or slave. Even if the son converts or is freed before the father dies, the bequest is still invalid. We will explore the reasoning behind this ruling, which hinges on the son's status as an heir at the time of the disposition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the types of dispositions covered
The passage covers acknowledgement (iqrār), will (wasiyyah), and gift (hibah) made on the deathbed. All are treated similarly.
2
Note the condition of the son at the time of disposition
The son is an infidel or a slave at the time the disposition is made. The disposition is made with a condition that the son may later convert or be freed.
The condition is part of the disposition formulation.
3
Determine the effect of subsequent conversion or freedom
Even if the son converts to Islam or is freed before the father's death, the disposition is invalid (bāṭil).
The text explicitly states: 'then the son accepts Islam before the father's death, all that is invalid.'
4
Understand the reason: suspicion of partiality
The invalidity is due to the existence of sonship at the time of the disposition. The son is an heir, and any bequest to an heir raises the suspicion of favoring, which is prohibited without other heirs' consent.
The text cites: 'because sonship exists at the time of acknowledgement, thus suspicion of preference is considered in inheritance.'
5
Confirm the Hanafi principle
The validity of a bequest depends on the status at the time of the act, not on subsequent changes. A later change does not cure the initial defect.
This principle applies similarly for both infidel and slave sons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كإقراره ووصيته وهبتِهِ لابنه كافراً أو عبداً إن أسلم أو أعتق بعد ذلك) : أي إن أقر المريض، أو أوصى، أو وهب لابنه الكافر ثم أسلم الابنُ قبل موتِ الآب بطل ذلك كله، أما الإقرار ؛ فلأن البنوة قائمة وقت الإقرار، فاعتبر في الإيراث تهمة الإيثار، وأما الهبة والوصيَّةُ فلما مرّ، فكذا إن كان الابن عبداً أو مكاتبا فعتق لما بينا.
Like his acknowledgement, will, and gift to his son who is an infidel or slave, if he accepts Islam or is freed after that. That is, if a sick person acknowledges, wills, or gives to his infidel son, then the son accepts Islam before the father's death, all that is invalid. As for acknowledgement, because sonship exists at the time of acknowledgement, thus suspicion of preference is considered in inheritance. As for gift and will, as previously. Similarly, if the son is a slave or a mukatab and then is freed, as we explained.
Source pages p. 587

Commentary
This passage addresses a deathbed bequest or gift to a son who is either a non-Muslim or a slave. The ruling is that such a disposition is invalid unless the son becomes a Muslim or is freed before the father's death. The reason given is the suspicion of partiality (tuhmat al-ithaar) because the son is an heir, and a bequest to an heir is not permitted without the consent of other heirs. Here, the son's conversion or freedom does not change the fact that at the time of the disposition, he was an heir. However, when the condition occurs before death, the disposition is considered valid as if made to a non-heir. The passage also mentions that the same applies if the son is a slave or mukatab (a slave under contract of manumission) who later gains freedom.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrār): Acknowledgement of a debt or right; here, a deathbed confession or acknowledgement
تهمة الإيثار (tuhmat al-īthār): Suspicion of favoring an heir over others in a bequest or gift
بطل (baṭala): To be invalid or void; the legal effect is that the disposition has no consequence
البنوة قائمة (al-bunuwwa qāʾima): Sonship exists; the relationship of son is present and acknowledged

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Invalid because of the suspicion of partiality.
C) Valid if it is from the one-third of the estate.
D) Invalid only if the other heirs object.

Key Takeaway
A bequest to an heir is invalid regardless of a subsequent change in the heir's status, because the determining factor is the status at the time of the bequest.

Gift from Chronically Ill Person: When from All Wealth vs. One-Third
Ruling on gift from chronically ill person

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on gifts from chronically ill persons and apply the condition that determines whether the gift is valid from all wealth or restricted to one-third.

Core Idea
A gift from a chronically ill person is from all wealth if the illness is prolonged and death not feared; otherwise from one-third.

In Hanafi fiqh, gifts made during illness receive careful scrutiny. The default rule for deathbed illness (marad al-mawt) restricts gifts to one-third of the estate, but what about chronic illnesses where death is not imminent? This lesson examines a passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah that clarifies the ruling for four specific chronic conditions: bedridden (maq'ad), paralyzed (mafluj), withered hand (ashall), and consumptive (maslul). Understanding the condition that triggers the one-third restriction is essential for correctly applying the law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The act is a gift (hiba) made by a person suffering from a chronic illness. Determine the basic ruling: the gift is valid (sahih) but may be restricted depending on circumstances.
The passage does not question the validity of the gift itself; the issue is its enforceability against the full estate.
2
Locate the condition that changes the ruling
The text states the gift is valid 'from all his wealth' if the illness is prolonged and he does not fear death. Otherwise, it is restricted 'from his third'. Identify the precise condition: 'if his illness is prolonged and he does not fear death' (in talat muddatuhu wa lam yakhaf mawtahu).
The fear of death must be subjective and reasonable, based on the illness's severity.
3
Understand the four types of chronic illness
The commentary defines four terms: maq'ad (bedridden, cannot stand due to disease), mafluj (paralyzed, half the body lost function), ashal (withered hand, paralyzed hand), and maslul (consumptive, with pus in chest). These are examples of chronic conditions that may or may not indicate imminent death.
These definitions help the jurist assess whether the illness is prolonged and whether death is feared.
4
Apply the condition to a case
Given a specific chronically ill person, determine: is the illness prolonged and does the patient fear death? If yes (prolonged and no fear), the gift is valid from all wealth. If no (short-term or fear present), only one-third.
The burden of proof lies on the one challenging the gift to show that death was feared.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصح هبة مقعد، ومفلوج، وأشلّ، ومسلول من كلِّ ماله إن طال مدته ولم يخف موته، وإلا فمن ثلثه. قوله : وصح هبة ... الخ ؛ المقعد : من لا يقدر على القيام لداء في جسده كأن الداء قعده، وعند الأطباء : هو الزَّمِن. والمفلوج : من ذهب نصفه وبطل عنه الحبس، ولا يقدر على الحركة. والأشل : مَن شلّت يده ؛ لأنَّ الشلل بالشين المعجمة آفة في اليد. والمسلول : بالسين المهملة، هو الذي به مرض السل، وهو عبارة عن اجتماع المدة في الصدر ونفسها. كذا في «الكوسجيّة». عن «الأكملية».
And a gift from a bedridden, paralyzed, one with a withered hand, and a consumptive is valid from all his wealth if his illness is prolonged and he does not fear death; otherwise, from his third. His statement: 'And a gift from a bedridden...' etc. The bedridden is one who cannot stand due to a disease in his body, as if the disease has seated him; according to physicians, it is the chronic. The paralyzed is one whose half has gone and the ability to hold is lost, and cannot move. The one with withered hand is one whose hand is paralyzed; because 'shalal' with the dotted shin is a defect in the hand. The consumptive, with the unpointed sin, is one who has the disease of consumption, which is the accumulation of pus in the chest and its breathing. As in al-Kusajiyya from al-Akmaliyya.
Source pages p. 587

Commentary
This passage deals with a gift made by a person suffering from a chronic disease (marad al-mawt is not necessarily present). The ruling: if the illness is prolonged and the person does not fear imminent death, the gift is considered valid from all his wealth (i.e., it is not restricted to one-third). However, if the illness is severe and death is feared, it is treated like a deathbed gift and restricted to one-third of the estate. The commentary defines the specific conditions: 'maq'ad' (bedridden), 'mafluj' (paralyzed), 'ashall' (withered hand), and 'maslul' (consumptive). These are examples of chronic illnesses that may or may not indicate imminent death, depending on duration and the patient's expectation.

Key Terms
مقعد (maq'ad): A person who cannot stand due to a chronic disease; bedridden.
مفلوج (mafluj): A person whose half of the body is paralyzed, unable to hold or move.
أشل (ashall): A person with a withered/paralyzed hand.
مسلول (maslul): A person suffering from consumption (tuberculosis), with pus accumulation in the chest.
مرض الموت (marad al-mawt): Deathbed illness that typically leads to death, restricting gifts to one-third.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) If he is above 60 years old
C) If the recipient is a relative
D) If the gift is in cash

Key Takeaway
A gift from a chronically ill person is enforced from all wealth only when the illness is prolonged and death is not feared; otherwise, it is restricted to one-third like a deathbed gift.

Priority Among Multiple Bequests When Estate is Insufficient
Ruling on order of execution when multiple bequests exceed one-third

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for prioritizing bequests when the one-third of the estate is insufficient, and apply it to distinguish between obligatory and voluntary bequests.

Core Idea
Obligatory bequests take precedence over voluntary ones, even if the testator mentioned them later.

When a person makes multiple bequests and the one-third share of the estate is not enough to fulfill all, the law must decide which bequests to execute first. The Hanafi ruling prioritizes obligatory religious duties over voluntary ones, regardless of the order in the will. This lesson explains the rule and its application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the types of bequests
Determine whether each bequest is obligatory (fard) like missed prayers, zakat, hajj, or voluntary (nafl) like supererogatory charity.
Obligatory bequests are those needed to fulfill missed personal obligations.
2
Apply the priority rule
If the one-third is insufficient and the bequests include both obligatory and voluntary, the obligatory bequests are fulfilled first, even if the testator mentioned them later in the will.
This overrides the testator's order of preference.
3
Handle equal-strength bequests
If all bequests are obligatory or all are voluntary, then the testator's own order of priority in the will is followed.
Equality in type requires respect for the testator's sequence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن اجتمع الوصايا قدّم الفرض وإن أخر. قوله : قدم الفرض؛ أي قدمه الموصي أو أخره كالزكاة والحج والصوم والصلاة. كذا في الجلبي. وإن تساوت قوة قدّم ما قدم) : أي إن اجتمع الوصايا، وضاق عنها ثلث المال ، فإن كان بعضها فرضاً وبعضُها نفلاً ، قدّم الفرض، وإن كان كلها فرائض، أو كلها نوافل قُدِّمَ ما قَدَّم الموصي. قوله : أو كلها نوافل؛ كالحج التطوع، والصدقة على الفقراء، أو كلّها واجب كالكفارات والنذور وصدقة الفطر. كذا في «العناية».
And if multiple bequests are gathered, the obligatory is given precedence even if he delayed it. His statement: 'gives precedence to the obligatory'; that is, whether the testator gave it precedence or delayed it, like zakat, hajj, fasting, prayer. As in al-Jalabi. And if they are equal in strength, he gives precedence to what he gave precedence. That is, if multiple bequests are gathered and the third of the wealth is insufficient for them, then if some are obligatory and some voluntary, the obligatory is given precedence. If all are obligatory or all are voluntary, what the testator gave precedence is given precedence. His statement: 'or all voluntary'; like voluntary hajj, charity to the poor, or all obligatory like expiations, vows, and zakat al-fitr. As in al-Inaya.
Source pages p. 587

Commentary
This passage addresses a scenario where a person's bequests exceed the one-third of the estate that is available for bequests. The order of priority is: first, bequests that are obligatory (fard) upon the testator, such as missed obligatory prayers, fasting, zakat, and hajj. These must be fulfilled even if the testator specified them later in the will. Second, if all bequests are of the same type (all obligatory or all voluntary), the testator's own order of priority in the will is followed. Voluntary bequests include charity and voluntary hajj, while obligatory bequests include expiations, nadhr (vows), and sadaqat al-fitr.

Key Terms
فرض (fard): Obligatory religious duty; here, bequests to fulfill missed obligations like prayer, fasting, zakat
نفل (nafl): Voluntary supererogatory act; here, voluntary bequests like charity

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person's bequests include both obligatory (fard) and voluntary (nafl) acts, and the one-third is insufficient, which is fulfilled first?
A) The one he mentioned first
B) The voluntary bequest
C) The obligatory bequest
D) The bequest with the largest amount

Key Takeaway
When the one-third of the estate is insufficient, obligatory bequests (like missed zakat, hajj, fasting) are fulfilled before voluntary ones (like charity), regardless of the testator's order; if all are of the same type, the testator's order is followed.

Bequest for Hajj: Performing from the Hometown and the Principle of Istihsan
General ruling on bequest for hajj from testator's hometown and exception for insufficient funds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the starting point for a bequest for hajj and explain the role of istihsan when funds are insufficient.

Core Idea
A bequest for hajj is performed from the testator’s hometown if funds suffice; otherwise from wherever they suffice – an exception to qiyas by istihsan.

This lesson examines the foundational rule for executing a bequest for hajj: the starting point of the journey. The Hanafi school holds that the hajj should be performed from the testator’s own hometown, reflecting the original obligation. But what happens if the bequest is insufficient for the full journey? The text introduces a key principle of istihsan that overrides strict analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its condition
The act is a bequest for hajj. The ruling states that the hajj is performed riding from the testator's hometown if the expense suffices. Note the implicit condition: the bequest amount must cover the cost from the hometown.
The term 'raakiban' (riding) reflects the usual mode; walking is not required.
2
Understand the basis: obligation from hometown
The reason given is that hajj is obligatory upon the testator from his hometown. Therefore the bequest is intended to fulfill that original obligation, so the starting point should be the same.
3
Recognize the exception when funds are insufficient
If the bequest does not cover the full journey from the hometown, the hajj is performed from wherever the funds suffice. This is a departure from strict analogy (qiyas), which would require the exact quality of the bequest.
4
Appreciate the role of istihsan
The text explicitly says 'qiyas would reject this' but we allow it by istihsan because the testator's intention was to gain reward. Executing the bequest partially is better than voiding it entirely.
Istihsan is a preference for a more equitable outcome over strict deduction.
5
Apply to a scenario
Consider a testator who leaves enough for hajj from a nearby city but not from his hometown. The proxy should start from that nearby city, using the available funds. This fulfills the bequest as much as possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Then if he bequeaths for hajj, one performs hajj on his behalf riding from his hometown if the expense suffices for that; otherwise from wherever it suffices. Then if a pilgrim dies on his way and bequeaths for hajj on his behalf, one performs hajj on his behalf from his hometown. He only said 'riding' because he is not obliged to walk; so it is directed to the manner that is obligatory upon him. As in al-Hidaya. His statement: 'otherwise'; that is, if the bequest does not cover the expense, he performs hajj on his behalf from wherever the bequest suffices. Source pages p. 588

Commentary
This passage addresses a bequest for hajj. The ruling: The hajj should be performed riding from the deceased's hometown if the bequest amount is sufficient; if not, from whichever place the bequest amount can cover the travel to. Then the text discusses a specific scenario: a person sets out for hajj, dies on the way, and had bequeathed for hajj to be performed on his behalf. According to Abu Hanifa, the hajj should be performed from his hometown (i.e., the starting point of his original journey) if the funds suffice; otherwise from where they suffice. According to the two students (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it should be performed from the place where he died. The justification for Abu Hanifa's view is that the deceased's hajj obligation remained from his hometown, so the bequest should fulfill it from there if possible. The students consider that since he already started the journey, the obligation is considered from the point of his death. The commentary also explains that the term 'raakiban' (riding) reflects the typical mode, and that if the bequest is insufficient for the full journey, it is executed to the extent possible, based on istihsan (juristic preference) over qiyas.

Key Terms
حج (hajj): The pilgrimage to Mecca; obligatory once in a lifetime for those who can afford it
وصية بالحج (wasiyyah bil-hajj): Bequest for hajj to be performed on behalf of the deceased
القياس يأباه (al-qiyas ya'bāhu): Analogy would reject this ruling; used to highlight that the ruling is based on istihsan

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The hajj is performed from the nearest city to Mecca.
C) The hajj is performed from wherever the bequest suffices.
D) The executor must add funds from the estate to cover the journey.

Key Takeaway
A bequest for hajj is performed from the testator's hometown if funds suffice; otherwise, by istihsan, from wherever the funds allow.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Starting Point When the Deceased Dies En Route
Intra-Hanafi dispute on starting point for hajj when the deceased dies en route.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the starting point for hajj on behalf of a deceased who died en route, and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
When a pilgrim dies en route and bequeaths for hajj, Abu Hanifa insists on the hometown, while the Sahibayn shift the start to the place of death — a dispute rooted in the nature of the obligation.

This lesson focuses on a specific scenario: a person sets out for hajj, dies on the way, and had bequeathed for hajj to be performed on his behalf. The Hanafi school is internally divided on the starting point. Abu Hanifa holds that the proxy should start from the deceased's hometown, while his two leading students (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue for starting from the place of death. We examine the reasoning behind each position and the practical difference it makes.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario
A man leaves his hometown as a pilgrim, dies en route, and had bequeathed for hajj on his behalf. The question: from where should the proxy commence the hajj?
The scenario assumes the bequest is valid and the proxy is appointed.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa (Imam al-Azam) rules that the hajj should be performed from the deceased's hometown, provided the bequest suffices; otherwise from wherever it suffices. This aligns with the general rule that the obligation originates from the hometown.
This is consistent with the earlier general ruling in the same passage.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the two companions) hold that the hajj should be performed from the place where the deceased died. Their reasoning: since he had already commenced the journey, the obligation is now attached to his location at death.
Their view is a departure from the general rule, specific to this scenario.
4
Analyze each proof
Abu Hanifa's proof: the original obligation is from the hometown; the bequest aims to fulfill that. The Sahibayn's proof: the journey's commencement shifts the obligation's locus to the point of death. The text does not provide full proofs but implies the reasoning.
The difference hinges on whether the obligation is considered 'fixed' at the hometown or 'mobile' after starting.
5
Identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
If the deceased died far from his hometown, performing hajj from the hometown may cost more. Under Abu Hanifa's view, the bequest might be insufficient; under the Sahibayn's view, the starting point is closer, likely reducing cost.
The practical effect also affects whether the bequest is executed partially or fully.

Source Text

Precedence of Bequests and the Hajj Bequest When Testator Dies en Route
Commentary explaining precedence rule and Hajj bequest dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for prioritizing bequests when the one-third is insufficient and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the location from which a deceased pilgrim's Hajj should be performed, evaluating the proofs of each side.

Core Idea
When bequests compete, obligatory ones come first; if equal, the testator's order dictates. For Hajj, the starting point is disputed: Abu Hanifah says home, Sahibayn say place of death.

This passage from the commentary on al-Hidayah addresses two interconnected issues: the general rule for prioritizing multiple bequests that exceed the one-third limit, and a specific scenario involving a bequest for Hajj when the testator dies en route. The commentator presents the default ruling for executing a Hajj bequest and then delves into an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifah and his two main disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) on the proper starting point for the substitute Hajj. Understanding this dispute requires analyzing the nature of the testator's journey and the role of intention in acts of worship.

Guided Path
1
Understand the general rule of precedence
When multiple bequests compete for the limited one-third of the estate, obligatory bequests (e.g., expiations, vows) take priority over supererogatory ones (e.g., voluntary Hajj, charity). If all bequests are of the same type, the testator's order of mention determines precedence.
2
Identify the default Hajj bequest ruling
If a testator bequeaths Hajj without having started his own journey, the substitute Hajj is performed riding from his town if the bequest covers the expense; otherwise from wherever the bequest suffices. This is based on the principle that Hajj is obligatory from one's residence.
3
Examine the dispute scenario
Consider a testator who sets out for Hajj, dies en route, and bequeaths that Hajj be performed for him. Abu Hanifah rules that the substitute Hajj starts from the testator's original town (if funds suffice), while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) rule that it starts from the place of death (if funds suffice).
4
Analyze Abu Hanifah's proof
Abu Hanifah analogizes to a merchant who dies on a business trip after bequeathing Hajj: since the merchant's journey was not an act of worship, the substitute Hajj starts from home. Similarly, the pilgrim died before completing the Hajj, so his outward journey is not considered an act of worship; thus the substitute must begin from his town.
5
Analyze Sahibayn's proof and the response
The Sahibayn argue that the pilgrim's journey, being undertaken with the intention of Hajj, is itself an act of worship. The distance covered fulfills part of the obligation, so the substitute should begin from where he died (if funds suffice), as if he were from that place. Abu Hanifah responds that intention alone does not make an incomplete journey an act of worship; istihsan favors starting from home to better fulfill the testator's presumed desire to have the entire Hajj performed from the heart of his locality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[٢] قوله : راكباً من بلده؛ لأنَّ الحج واجب على الموصي من بلده، ولذا يعتبر فيه من المال لا يكفيه من بلده، والوصية لأداء ما هو الواجب عليه، وإنما قال : راكباً؛ لأنه لا يلزمه أن يحج ماشياً، فانصرف إليه على الوجه الذي وجـب عـلـيـه. كذا في «الهداية» [٣] قوله : وإلا؛ أي وإن لم تبلغ الوصية النفقة حجّ عنه من حيث تبلغ الوصية، والقياس يأباه؛ لأنه أمر بالحجّة على صفةٍ عدمناها فيه، لكنا جوزناه؛ لأنا نعلم أنّ الموصي قصد تنفيذ الوصيّة للثواب، فيجب تنفيذها مهما أمكن، والممكن فيه ما ذكرنا من شرط بلوغ النفقة، وهو أولى من إبطالها برأسها. كذا في «الهداية». [٤] قوله : فإن مات ... الخ؛ صورة المسألة : خرج رجل من بلده حاجاً فمات في طريقه وأوصى بالحج عنه يحج عنه من بلده إن بلغ نفقة ذلك، وإن لم تبلغ، فمن حيث تبلغ النفقة، هذا عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما: يحج عنه من حيث مات. عند أبي حنيفة " إن بلغ نفقته ذلك، وإلا فمن حيث تبلغ، وعندهما يحج من حيث مات، وإن لم تبلغ النفقة ذلك، فمن حيث تبلغ. [١]قوله : عند أبي حنيفة له؛ له : إنَّ الوصية تنصرف إلى الحج من بلده؛ لأنه لما مات قبل إتمام العمل صار خروجه بغير الحج كما إذا خرج تاجراً فمات في بعض الطريق يحج من منزله، فكذلك هاهنا. ولهما : إنَّ السفر نية الحج، وقع قربةً وسقط فرضُ قطع المسافة بقدر سفره، وقد وقع أجره على الله، فيبتدئ من مكان مات فيه كأنه من أهله، بخلاف سفر التجارة؛ لأنه لم يقع قربة، فيحج عنه من بلده، كذا في بعض حواشي «الهداية».
And if they are equal in strength, he gives precedence to what he gave precedence. So if he bequeaths Hajj, Hajj will be performed on his behalf riding from his town if the bequest covers its expenses, otherwise from wherever it suffices. Then if a pilgrim dies on his way and bequeaths Hajj to be performed on his behalf, it will be performed on his behalf from his town. 'And if they are equal in strength, he gives precedence to what he gave precedence' meaning: if multiple bequests are combined and the one-third of the estate is insufficient for all, then if some are obligatory and some supererogatory, the obligatory is given precedence. If all are obligatory or all are supererogatory, what the testator gave precedence to is given precedence. So if he bequeaths Hajj, Hajj will be performed on his behalf riding from his town if the bequest covers its expenses, otherwise from wherever it suffices. Then if a pilgrim dies on his way and bequeaths Hajj to be performed on his behalf, it will be performed on his behalf from his town: i.e., Hajj is performed from his town. [1] He said: 'or all are supererogatory,' like voluntary Hajj and charity to the poor, or all are obligatory like expiations, vows, and Fitr charity. As in al-`Inayah. [2] He said: 'riding from his town' because Hajj is obligatory on the testator from his town, so consideration is given to whether the estate suffices from his town, and the bequest is to perform what is obligatory on him. He said 'riding' because he is not required to walk, so it is directed to the manner that was obligatory on him. As in al-Hidayah. [3] He said: 'otherwise,' i.e., if the bequest does not cover the expense, Hajj will be performed on his behalf from wherever the bequest suffices. Analogy (qiyas) rejects this because it is a command for Hajj in a manner that we are unable to fulfill, but we permit it because we know that the testator intended to execute the bequest for reward, so it must be executed as much as possible. The possible here is what we mentioned of the condition of expenses being sufficient, and this is better than invalidating it entirely. As in al-Hidayah. [4] He said: 'then if he dies...' The substance of the issue: A man left his town as a pilgrim and died on the way, and bequeathed that Hajj be performed on his behalf. It will be performed from his town if the bequest covers that expense; if not, then from wherever the expense is sufficient. This is according to Abu Hanifah. According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it will be performed from where he died. According to Abu Hanifah, if the bequest covers the expense, otherwise from wherever it suffices. According to them, it is performed from where he died, and if the expense does not cover that, then from wherever it suffices. [1] He said: 'According to Abu Hanifah, his evidence: The bequest is directed to Hajj from his town because when he died before completing the action, his departure became non-Hajj, just as if he had left as a merchant and died on the way, Hajj would be performed from his home. So here too. According to them: The journey with the intention of Hajj was an act of worship, and the obligation to cover the distance fell to the extent of his travel, and his reward is with Allah, so it begins from the place where he died as if he were from its people, unlike a business trip because it was not an act of worship. So Hajj is performed on his behalf from his town. As in some glosses of al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 588

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory commentary on the rule of precedence when several bequests compete for the one-third of the estate. The commentator first states the general principle: when bequests are equal in strength (all obligatory or all supererogatory), the one the testator mentioned first is given precedence. This is demonstrated through examples: if some are obligatory (like expiations) and others supererogatory (like voluntary Hajj), the obligatory is given precedence. The detailed example then turns to a bequest for Hajj. The default ruling is that the Hajj is performed riding from the testator's town if the bequest covers the expense; otherwise from wherever the bequest suffices. This is because Hajj is obligatory from one's residence. The commentator then introduces a scenario where the testator himself set out for Hajj, died en route, and bequeathed that Hajj be performed for him. This leads to an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifah holds that the Hajj should be performed from the testator's town (if the bequest covers it), analogizing his journey to that of a merchant whose journey is not an act of worship. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that the journey with the intention of Hajj was itself an act of worship, so the obligation to cover the distance has been fulfilled to the extent of the travel; hence the Hajj should begin from the place of death. The commentator supports Abu Hanifah's view with istihsan (juristic preference), overriding qiyas, to fulfill the testator's intent as far as possible.

Key Terms
وصية (wasiyyah): A bequest made by a testator to be executed after death from up to one-third of the estate.
حج (hajj): The pilgrimage to Makkah; here it refers to performing it on behalf of a deceased person as an act of charity or fulfilling a vow.
ثلث المال (thuluth al-mal): One-third of the testator's estate after debts, which can be disposed of through bequests.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) From the place where he died
B) From his town of origin
C) From the nearest point to Makkah
D) Not performed at all because he already intended Hajj

Key Takeaway
When a testator dies en route to Hajj after bequeathing it, the Hanafi school's relied-upon view (Abu Hanifah) is that the substitute Hajj begins from his home town if the bequest covers the expense; if not, from wherever it suffices.
